==PAGE:1==
Maturity Model for Strong Owner
Capabilities Development in the
Construction Industry
AUTHORS
Ali El Chaabi
Nikolaos Anevlavis
SUPERVISOR
Christian Thuesen
February 12, 2022
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
Today, there is a known crisis in the construction industry worldwide where major projects
and programs are experiencing substantial cost increases and long overruns. This is mainly due to
the lack of skills and experience needed to set up successful projects or programs from the outset.
This research study aims to investigate the way construction is being delivered and how owner
organizations can be transformed by developing strong owner capabilities focusing on three di-
mensions: strategic, commercial, and governance capabilities. This study made a 3-dimensional
capability mapping based on recent literature and interviews with industry practitioners. The
developed capability map and the findings from the literature and interviews included capabil-
ities, critical success factors, industry challenges, and best practices. This based the foundation
for a conceptual maturity model that aims to help organizations within the industry assess their
maturity level, which is intended to aid them in road mapping their sustainable transformational
journey towards SDGs 9 and 11, by connecting people, organizations, data, and technology.
I dag er der en kendt krise i byggebranchen verden over, hvor store projekter og program-
mer oplever betydelige omkostningsstigninger og lange overskridelser. Dette skyldes hoved-
sageligt manglen på kompetencer og erfaringer, der er nødvendige for at levere kvalitet bygge
projekter eller programmer. Denne forskningsundersøgelse har til formål at undersøge, hvordan
byggeri bliver leveret, og hvordan bygherrer kan transformeres ved at udvikle stærke bygherre
kapabiliteter med fokus på tre dimensioner: strategiske, kommercielle og governace evner. Denne
undersøgelse lavede en 3-dimensionel kompetemcekortlægning baseret på nyere litteratur og in-
terviews med branche eksperter. Det udviklede kapabilitetskort og resultaterne fra litteraturen og
interviewene omfattede de kompetencer, kritiske succesfaktorer, brancheudfordringer og bed-
ste praksis. Dette baserede grundlaget for en konceptuel modenhedsmodel, der har til formål at
hjælpe organisationer inden for industrien med at vurdere deres modenhedsniveau, som har til
formål at hjælpe dem med at kortlægge deres bæredygtige transformationsrejse mod SDGs 9 og
11 ved at forbinde mennesker, organisationer, data , og teknologi.

TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
1
Introduction
1.1
Industry Background
Constructions whether it is buildings or infrastructure from bridges to roads, play an impor-
tant role at the very core of the economic and social development. They provide the foundation
for almost all modern economic activities, constitute an important economic sector in their unique
way, and contribute significantly to increase living standards and quality of life (Barrie, Pierre-
Alain, & Michel, 2006). Moreover, construction are a natural part of everyday life. Every year,
professional owner organizations initiate construction renovation of buildings and construction
in Denmark to an estimated value of up to 100 billion DKK (Bang, Busch, Simonsen, & Ul-
lum, 2016). It is the owner who makes the earliest decisions in the construction process. Thus,
they play a central role in shaping the building and construction projects that significantly affect
society. It applies right from our housing, leisure, community, transport, communication, and
supply. The owner has a key position in ensuring that all interests are taken into consideration
and that the parties in the construction work together to meet the project’s objectives. To succeed,
it requires owners to navigate the board of directors and ask the right questions to consultants
and contractors to better manage projects, processes, and actors through everyone involved in
the project. However, despite constructions importance to society, and the vital role of owner
organizations in them, the current practices of constructions’ delivery are not living up to expec-
tations. (Flyvbjerg, Holm, & Buhl, 2002; Changali, Mohammad, & Nieuwland, 2015; Roehrich,
2020a).
1.2
State of the Art
According to several sources, construction projects are delivered behind schedule, beyond the
cost estimate and fail to meet the public’s expectations (Changali et al., 2015; Roehrich, 2020a).
Traditional research on mega projects explains this by strategic misrepresentation (Flyvbjerg et al.,
2002), where benefits of projects are overestimated while budgets are underestimated and suggest
concepts like reference class forecasting (Flyvbjerg, 2008) as remedial strategies. While these
1
==PAGE:16==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
are acknowledged as important contributions they fail to fundamentally address the underlying
causes of increasing complexity and uncertainty (Love, Edwards, & Irani, 2012). Now more than
ever, there is a significant need to improve construction delivery (Flyvbjerg, 2008; Love et al.,
2012). By taking that into account many recent research and industry initiatives reviewed in this
dissertation, suggest a plethora of strategies focused on the organizational capabilities to improve
the performance of the construction delivery.
One of the main sources that based this research study was the owner capability framework
developed by (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016). The capability framework was framed in three
pillars or the so called dimensions. The first one is the strategic capabilities dimension, which
are the activities that are the full responsibility of the owner organization as they relate to the
business and corporate strategy of the organization. The second one is the commercial capabilities
dimension, which are the capabilities needed in order for the owner organization to collaborate
and work with its suppliers. The last dimension is the governance capabilities, which are mainly
those organizational competencies required in order for a strong owner organization to interface
successfully with a temporary project organization (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) and to support
the interface between the projects (Sergeeva, 2019).
It is worth noting that the role of owner organization in construction delivery in Scandinavia
was well described in (Bang et al., 2016). According to that research, owner organizations rep-
resent a variety of different organizations. One could distinguish between public, supported, and
private owners in the construction sector. Moreover, all owner organizations in Denmark com-
ply with rules such as environmental legislation, working environment legislation, the planning
act and they use the AB system as a contractual basis for cooperation with the companies in the
delivery system. In addition, some owners are subject to several frameworks, which contributes to
their work nature. One of the main differences in the framework conditions in the procurement
legislation that is common to the public owners, but which must not be complied with by the
private ones. These rules are fundamental for how the procurement process itself takes place in
relation to the collaboration with the delivery system. In addition, there is no specific education
leading to an owner profession in the Danish education system. It is solely experience-based and
through privately offered courses that one can obtain knowledge about the role of the owner.
2
==PAGE:17==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
The owner’s Association in Denmark was established in 1999 at the request of the Ministry of
Housing, which wanted the owners to have a standard forum for the exchange of experiences. In
Sweden, the United Kingdom and the Netherlands similar organizations were established, how-
ever otherwise, not many examples of professional organization are known of the owner role.
The role of owner organization in Denmark was reviewed in this research.
1.3
Thesis Objectives
This goal of this research study was to create a theoretical framework for Strong Owner
capability development that owner organizations in the construction industry can use for capa-
bility maturity assessment. In this research study various recent academic and industry articles
were reviewed to build on (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) framework. Moreover empirical data
was gathered and analyzed, to further develop the framework and build a maturity assessment
tool to measure the performance of each capability at owner organizations. The first step was
to conduct an initial research analysis of the needed capabilities in the industry and outline the
current practices. The second step was collecting empirical qualitative data from several owner
organizations through semi-structured interviews. Based on the gathered data from the literature
and the interviews, a capability map was created in order to define and visualize the needed capa-
bilities for an organization to become strong. In addition to that, a conceptional framework of a
maturity model for SO capabilities was developed in order to address the industry-based problem.
The framework focus aims to identify the already available strengths, weaknesses, and potential
improvement areas that would be the basis for organizations to grow. As a result, the thesis main
objectives were defined as the following:
• Identify and critically reflect upon key owner capabilities, challenges, and best practices of
a strong owner organization for construction delivery.
• Identify and prioritize capabilities, critical success factors, maturity elements, and devel-
opment enablers to improve construction delivery by finding relevant and supplementary
information from internal and external sources necessary to solve the challenge and docu-
ment best practices.
3
==PAGE:18==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
• Qualitative assessment of collected data from interviews with industry practitioners.
• Development of an owner organization capability map.
• Development of an owner organization capability maturity assessment tool.
1.4
Problem Statement
Today there is a known crisis in the industry in general around the world where major
construction programs are experiencing huge cost increases and long overruns (Atkins, 2021).
There are a lot of reasons why the best laid plans fail. However, they usually lead back to a
few common problems. First is the lack of capabilities of skills and experience which are needed
to set up successful projects and programmes. Second reason is the slow decision-making pro-
cess with and inaccurate baselines and expectations of time, scope, and budget. Third reason is
the poor and often out of date information flows from projects. Considering the data, available
in the industry, the construction industry is behind others in terms of modernising the way it
manages and delivers major programmes. The car industry is an exemplar that has embraced all
opportunities to transform its delivery of projects and programmes by sharing data, improving
collaboration, and changing core skills and behaviours – both internally and in the supply chain.
As a result, the industry can achieve more consistent and predicable outcomes which have in-
creased quality, productivity, and outputs. Inspired by other industries successful transformation
the construction industry recognises that they must change to achieve better projects and pro-
grammes performance (Atkins, 2021). The following research questions were raised to tackle the
problem statement.
Research Question 1: What are the capabilities required by owner organizations in the con-
struction industry to execute projects more effectively and efficiently?
Research Question 2: How can owner organizations in the construction industry assess their
capability performance in order to improve their performance?
4
==PAGE:19==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
This study made a 3-dimensional capability mapping based on recent literature and inter-
views with industry practitioners. The developed capability map and the findings from the lit-
erature and interviews included capabilities, critical success factors, industry challenges, and best
practices. This based the foundation for a conceptional maturity model that aims to help orga-
nizations within the industry assess their maturity level, which is intended to aid them in road
mapping their sustainable transformational journey towards SDGs 9 and 11 , by connecting peo-
ple, organizations, data, and technology.

Report Structure
In this section, the report structure was described to facilitate the reading process for the
reader. First, the Introduction of this thesis report covered an industry background, and a state of

current academic and empirical knowledge necessary for the research area was introduced and

2
Literature Review
This literature review studies the existing knowledge and best practices from academia and
industry about organizational capabilities covering three dimensions: strategic, commercial, and
governance. In addition, criteria or the so-called critical success factors (CSFs) and enabling ca-
pability dimensions were reviewed. More specifically, this chapter provides an overview of pre-
viously published work, focusing primarily on the dissertation title: Maturity Model for Strong
Owner Capability Development in the Construction Industry. Likewise, relevant theories, mod-
els, and ideas defining the topic and determining the direction of the research were presented.
It is worth noting that the conceptual models, key definitions, concepts, and best practices were
described in relation to the analysis, results, recommendations, and further discussions. In con-
clusion, the literature review is related to this dissertation’s research objectives and questions.
2.1
Capabilities Defined
Following (Winter, 2003) the term of organizational capability on the broader concept was
defined as an organizational routine. Winter argues that an organizational capability is a high-
level routine (or collection of routines) that, when put together with the correct input, can bring
a set of decisions options to the management that can produce significant output for the organi-
zation. Winter emphasizes different types of routines learned behavior that is highly patterned. It
is worth noting that the primary research focuses on shedding light on the capabilities required
by owner organizations in the construction industry to execute projects more effectively and ef-
ficiently, and how owner organizations in the construction industry can assess their capability
effectiveness in order to improve their performance. In the next section, the organizational ca-
pabilities needed for an owner organization to become a strong owner in the construction sector
were presented, and the correlation between them was examined.
7
==PAGE:22==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
2.2
Capabilities of the Strong Owner Concept in the Construction Sector
Defining the Strong Owner terminology is significantly essential, which describes mature
and experienced organizations within internal organizational capabilities. This terminology is
primarily associated with the independence of an organization to operate effectively and effi-
ciently, either alone or in collaboration with other organizations under its leadership. Thus, a
strong owner organization is an organization that is capable of executing a project, service, or
producing a product by including its value chain and assets intelligently without being dependent
on an asset supplier. Based on the conceptual framework of (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016), the
capabilities needed for an organization to expand its skills and become a strong organization can
be assigned in-between three dimensions of capabilities which are; strategical, commercial, and
governance. In this section, the capabilities under these three dimensions were reviewed.
Winch’s framework focuses on the capabilities required by the owner organization for the ac-
quisition of construction assets in order to extend or improve its capabilities. The framework was
created mainly for infrastructure development, involving practitioners from the Swedish trans-
port administration, and tested in Denmark, Sweden, Norway, and the Netherlands. Therefore,
it is highly considered as an essential conceptual framework for capability development in the
construction sector in the Nordics. In order to test if the model is sustainable for the Danish con-
struction industry, this research focus was considered. It is noted that the research was focused on
challenging, revising, and further developing Winch and Leiringer’s conceptual model in order
to validate and adjust it to the requirements of the Danish construction sector. In addition, a
maturity assessment model will be developed to assess the maturity of organizational capabilities
in the construction sector.
A recent study that was done by the union of client (owner) organizations in Denmark (Bang
et al., 2016) conveys a preliminary project based on interviews with a range of industry profes-
sionals and experts unfolding the complexity of the owner organization activities. The report
examines what is needed to develop owner organizational capabilities to increase value creation
from projects in the Danish market. This was done through an initial analysis and comprehensive
mapping of competencies necessary for owner organizations to face their challenges. Further-
8
==PAGE:23==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
more, the report provided preliminary suggestions on how the capabilities can be built, what it
takes to succeed with intensifying capabilities development, and leaping to a more prominent
owner role. The goal was to present ideas with concrete initiatives that can positively affect this
very central field. The following capability map presented in the figure 2 was the output of the
study.
Figure 2: Capabilities based on Bang et al., 2016
In the next section, with the offset of the strong owner conceptual model (G. Winch &
Leiringer, 2016), a definition of the three capability dimensions were introduced. According
to (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016), these categorized owner capability dimensions are; strategic,
commercial, and governance capabilities. This section reviewed each category considering the
capabilities included in Winch and Leiringer’s framework as a starting point. In addition, ca-
pabilities from other research and studies among the scientific and industrial community were
introduced.
2.2.1
Strategic Capabilities
Strategic capabilities are defined, controlled, and owned by the owner organization (G. Winch
& Leiringer, 2016). These capabilities are activities that are the full responsibility of the owner
organization as they relate to the business and corporate strategy of the organization. Summing
9
==PAGE:24==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
up, only the owner organization alone can relate the investment project benefits to the upper
strategy. Thus, project capabilities are capabilities needed in an owner organization to achieve a
successful outcome in a project. The projects’ primary goal should be to achieve a desirable out-
come and benefit the organization while implementing its vision through its mission and strategy.
It is worth noting that Winch and Leiringer identified capabilities that are not typically seen as
project management capabilities under the strategic capability domain among other researchers.
For example, they shed light on why some projects get funding and are prioritized over others.
In addition, they focused on construction projects as many different services are provided in this
area. Typically, the organizations that own and manage them have many competitive investment
opportunities to use and utilize their resource base. So how do they choose which one to go with
and what to do with projects and programs?
Capital raising depicts an organization’s ability to raise the capital to fund its investment
projects. This capability is unquestionably an essential owner capability. The owners who lack
capital raising skills cannot raise sufficient economic sources to invest, leading to unsustainable
projects. In connection to that, if there are not enough funds to support the investment, then
the project most likely will fail (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016). In the public sector, the processes
for capital raising are not the same as the private sector. In the public sector nowadays, raising
funding to support investments in construction upgrades is significantly more default than before.
In order to provide new sources of funding for essential projects, governments have turned to
public-private partnerships (Hodge, Greve, & Boardman, 2010). That has mainly occurred due
to the given reluctance of voters to pay higher and higher taxes, the high economic dept, and the
growing demands of prosperity (Wolfgang, 2011).
Project portfolios are projects sponsored by the same organization which shares scarce re-
sources but do not have any technical dependencies" (G. M. Winch, 2009). According to (Bower,
1972) the primary resource is the financial capital, which means the project portfolio manage-
ment is correlated with capital budgeting. At this point, Bower argues that there are many issues,
and the investment appraisal is one of them. Following that, (Penrose, 2009) highlighted a crucial
factor for the growth of an organization, where (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) translated the
growth of an organization into the strategy of the organization is the way of managing the re-
10
==PAGE:25==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
sources internally. He also claimed that if an organization decides to outsource these management
resources, it can be challenging because it requires an introduction to its internal rules, culture,
ethics, and practices. Following on that, (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) suggests that stakeholder
management has a crucial role to play in the strategic level of the owner organization, as if omit-
ted, it could hinder the development of the business. In addition, they emphasize the importance
of investment projects for the prosperity and development of the organization as it is largely
linked to securing financial resources for upcoming projects. On top of that, (Gottlieb, Thuesen,
Frederiksen, & Berg, 2020) goes further to analyze the strategic capabilities from the conceptional
framework proposed by (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) by conducting interviews with ByK. ByK
is an owner organization in Denmark and has made strategic partnership agreements with Trust
and DSP Plus. (Gottlieb et al., 2020) found that by far the most critical strategic capability on
the owner’s side is considered to be the ability to manage the project portfolio. This capability
is considered the most important, especially at ByK, and from the partnerships side. In particu-
lar, survey respondents with specific partnership experience assess that portfolio management is
the most central owner capability in working with strategic partnerships. This is understand-
able, as the strategic partnerships are built around a specific portfolio. For example, a respondent
in TRUST states that portfolio management is crucial for a strategic partnership, mainly when
operating with a project office. Portfolio management thus becomes a matter of being able to
see the long term and adapt resources accordingly. However, (Gottlieb et al., 2020) found that
owners are not experienced in working with long-term construction management projects. This
controversial finding creates space for further research; thus, in this research, a maturity assess-
ment will be developed for Danish owner organizations in the construction industry to assess the
maturity level of their capabilities.
Project selection refers to the activities required for an owner organization to effectively pri-
oritize and allocate its funds and capital to select which project should be implemented. Herein,
the research focus should illuminate the conditions where a project can be selected among the
rest to receive enough funds and capital in order to be sustainable. In addition, (G. Winch &
Leiringer, 2016) argue that there is a need for further research in the project management liter-
ature on investment evaluation processes since, for many years, the project selection capabilities
11
==PAGE:26==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
did not get enough attention from the researchers. The project portfolio’s lack of attention from
the researchers mainly occurred due to the researchers’ perception that these skills should not be
part of the scope of the project management field as they were considered part of a more tech-
nical skill. (Flyvbjerg, Bruzelius, & Rothengatter, 2003) were some of the researchers who tried
and succeeded in changing this perception. They shed light on why these skills should be part
of the project management field. They come up with the so-called terminology about strategic
misrepresentation on the investment evaluation processes. By that, they mean the inappropriate
and incompliant behavior of the organization when it comes to selecting projects for the pipeline.
Also, they pointed out two main concepts on why strategic misrepresentation may occur. The
first concept is related to the poor faith of organizational phenomena in which incentives in-
fluence evaluation. While the second one is a psychological phenomenon in which cognitive
limitations lead to biased evaluations (G. M. Winch, 2009). On the one hand, the organization
may deliberately misinform about the actual costs and benefits of investing in the project in order
to secure sufficient funds in any case. On the other hand, referring to the (Weinstein, 1980) the
organization has the natural tendency to be optimistic and look at the glass half full.
The project’s mission indicates a clear definition of the project’s goals, purpose, and objec-
tives. An owner organization can define and set up the project’s critical success factors. According
to (Gottlieb et al., 2020) defining a project’s mission creates both clarities in the project organiza-
tion and helps to identify possible paths to make the project a success. As an owner organization,
it is clear to create the financial basis for a project to be completed. This can be done by provid-
ing guarantees about investment funds, future income, or the like. Also, it is crucial to consider
ways of comparing the optimal metrics to determine the appropriate targets for the project, e.g.,
cost-benefit analysis. In addition to that, (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) addressed an underlined
problem when the organization cannot define and describe the objectives of the project precisely.
The issue concerns the strategic misinterpretation of the project mission by the stakeholders who
may want to change the scope according to their interests. Therefore, the owner must handle the
most critical stakeholders and secure they are all on board and the on same page. For instance, a
delivery team, like engineering consultancies, also has strategic capabilities, but these are slightly
different; they should meet the owner’s needs, and the owner should know how to manage them.
12
==PAGE:27==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
This strategic capability is about creating a long-term mission that the company can implement.
Nevertheless, the project’s mission should not focus only on the financial aspects of the project.
For example, the cost-benefit analysis reflected low economic benefits on the UK’s High Speed 2
railway line project. However, it was a tremendous symbolical political movement since this line
would connect the North with the South part of the UK and bring the people and companies
closer together (Overman, 2013). As a result, there are several factors to needs to be considered
in an owner organization of a particular size before defining the company’s mission, leading to
the project’s optimal performance.
Stakeholder management refers to maintaining good relationships with the people who have
the most influence and interest in the project. Managing stakeholders is identified as a vital owner
capability. (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) define stakeholder management as the key to project
management and that it is the owner’s responsibility to handle it effectively. In addition, ac-
cording to the findings of (Gottlieb et al., 2020), they have found that partnerships indicate that
this capability is vital on the part of the owner. Although, according to their findings, it has
not been identified as particularly important by ByK. This may indicate that the partnerships
experience a great need for effective management of stakeholders from the owner’s side. Also,
standard stakeholder management techniques for handling stakeholders often use a thematic anal-
ysis. (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) stretch out that there are limitations on this approach. The
main arguments address that the organization that focuses on the stakeholders and their interest
"tends to emphasize agency in both stakeholder actions and owner responses." In contrast, they
suggest that the organization should focus only on the institutional embeddedness between the
owner and the inter-organization, resulting in a more in-depth view of the structural constraints
on the project and the agents’ efforts to overcome them.
All the capabilities mentioned above provide different values from a private to a public organi-
zation. According to the findings of (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016), the construction agencies can
equally realize the importance of the strategic capabilities for the public organization to execute
the project. However, the existence of these possibilities seems to be very difficult, as politicians
and consequently the government largely control the public body. In addition, they stressed the
importance of early decision-making processes that significantly impact the project’s upcoming
13
==PAGE:28==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
performance; it is also significantly politicized. That means there is insufficient room for the pub-
lic organization to work independently to execute the project since their influence is minimal.
They also argued that the politician might have a different view of the success factors of a project
from the owner organization. It can be seen in their study that the politicians were responsible
for the selection, the definition of the object, the purpose, and the goal of the whole project,
which is then delivered to the owner organization. This indicates that project performance is not
included in this delivery arrangement, so it has little influence on the decisions made. In addition,
the study showed that the hierarchical relationship between the organization and the govern-
ment has a significant effect on the skills required to operate the owner’s organization. Their
study found that "only managing the project portfolio of the generic investment opportunities
lies" is their responsibility. Nevertheless, even this is "affected by national investment plans and
the availability of funds throughout the project life-cycle" (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016).
Social responsibility has recently filled much of the construction industry debate according
to (Bang et al., 2016). There is a focus on the finished constructions and facilities that create the
most benefit for the users. So far, constructions may also be future-proofed for new usage patterns
while looking at how they support the solution of societal challenges. However, in the execution,
the owners should have an ethical responsibility to ensure proper working conditions for those
involved to realize the projects. This applies to the material side but also on the construction sites.
Challenges with social dumping and foreign labor with unacceptable employment conditions
have filled much debate in recent years. Furthermore, (Bang et al., 2016) states that the working
environment and safety are key issues on construction sites. Also, accident rates have not dropped
in construction over the past 30 years. In an attempt to crack the curve, the owner’s safety and
health rules have been significantly tightened. Accidents happen, but part of the cause can be
found in the inadequate planning of projects. In summary, a remarkable feature of the literature
for the project strategy is that it deals with current research in strategic management and the
theoretical developments in strategy as a practice.
Following the research questions and purpose, the aforementioned capabilities were clustered
under the strategic dimension. It is worth noting that the project mission definition and project
selection were considered critical success factors under the project portfolio management since

TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
they coherently relate to this capability, defining an owner organization’s strategy. The following
table summarizes the strategic capabilities needed for an owner organization to become a strong
owner organization.
Strategic Capabilities
Capital Raising
Project Portfolio Management
Stakeholder Management
Environmental, Social, and Governance (ESG)
Table 1: Strategic Capabilities Based on Literature
2.2.2
Commercial Capabilities
Commercial capabilities refer to the capabilities needed for an owner organization to inter-
face with its suppliers. This can happen in many different ways. According to (National Au-
dit Office, 2010), owners’ commercial capabilities have a crucial role to play in the delivery of
assets, where many organizations, especially public organizations, lack these skills.
According to (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) the first commercial capability is packaging.
Packaging capabilities focus on the capabilities needed to separate large work packages into smaller
packages or combine smaller packages into larger packages to make them more attractive for sup-
pliers to bid on. The capabilities are highly linked with the work breakdown structure, which
completes complex projects with many moving parts step-by-step. Packaging capabilities play an
important role for an owner organization, as they support the organization early in defining their
contracting strategy. It is worth noting that packages for external contracts require a high de-
gree of coordination. It is essential to clearly define the owner’s responsibilities over the suppliers
to avoid confusion and breach. In order to do so, the owner organization must understand and
define their needs first. On one side of the coin, the owner owns the activities related to the inter-
faces between packages. On the other side of the coin, the activities related inside of the packages
lie on the supplier side. According to the findings of (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016), packaging
15
==PAGE:30==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
capabilities are part of the commercial dimension since commercial agreements can be created
for what suppliers or vendors can supply, and therefore highlights the contracting competencies.
The second commercial capability according to (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) is procure-
ment. Procurement capabilities indicate the capabilities needed to identify, evaluate, select, and
inspire suppliers. These capabilities give owners the competencies to work with suppliers to sup-
port and ensure a high level of market competition. The major responsibilities of the owner are
to ensure transparent tendering processes, desirable and fair deals, and design sufficient rewards
that are attractive for the suppliers both in terms of financial and behavioral aspects. (G. Winch
& Leiringer, 2016) argue that the researchers tended to shed light on the nature of the trans-
action of the contracting capabilities, so they are very well defined and developed in that sense.
Although, there is still room for further research and improvement in contracting strategy ca-
pabilities. Winch and Leiringer point out the following areas where the researchers among the
academia and the industry should set the focus on for further investigation: "the capabilities of the
supply chain, the timing about the economic cycle, and a wide variety of issues in the institutional
environment such as public sector procurement regulations." (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016)
In addition, many studies identify the importance of procurement-related capabilities. For
example, (van den Hurk & Verhoest, 2015) argue that the selection of the appropriate tendering
processes and selection criteria is an essential part of these capabilities. In addition, (T. Liu &
Wilkinson, 2014; Kujala, Aaltonen, Gotcheva, & Lahdenperä, 2020) support that these criteria
should focus on a wide range including "ranging from price-based bidding to value and ability-
based selection procedures." For the organization to motivate and enhance its teams with skills
and capabilities needed for better project performance, (Davies, MacAulay, DeBarro, & Thurston,
2014; Guo, Chang-Richards, Wilkinson, & Li, 2014) propose that organizational competencies
can be enhanced by systematic training and ongoing learning. However, they also believe these
methods have a few drawbacks related to the high cost. On the one hand, focusing on temporary
organizations, these enablers can be highly costly and ineffective since the high rate of employee
turnover. Although, by having strategic partnerships with the preferred suppliers or key actors,
the cost can be decreased. On the other hand, focusing on an owner organization is significantly
more effective, especially in the long run, creating a vast amount of savings due to the nominal
16
==PAGE:31==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
rate of employee turnover.
Moreover, according to (Bang et al., 2016) authority and legal processing is an important
competence an owner organization should possess. The term ”Authority and Legal Processing”
defines the owner’s involvement in facilitating authority approvals for projects. In order to do
so, that requires that the owner needs to have knowledge and involvement in the latest tender
regulations. Moreover, the owner’s role in facilitating the approval of legal and authority matters
of the project is vital when it comes to project execution.
The third commercial capability according to (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) is relational.
Relational capabilities refer to the capabilities needed for the organization to collaborate with
commercial parties effectively. Traditionally, the desired relationships between the parties are
stated under a formal and legal agreement in-between them. The most common legal agreement
used in industry is the contractual agreement. Behavioral relationships are defined in terms of
conventions that represent the requirements and wishes of all parties. However, many researchers
found that contracts sometimes cannot serve their original purpose. (Poppo & Zenger, 2002; Gil,
Pinto, & Smyth, 2011) argue that interpersonal relationships are the basis for practical cooper-
ation between the parties. Thus, contracts can be used to frame business behaviors in addition
to behavioral relationships. Based on the findings of (N. Caldwell & Howard, 2010) within the
area of procuring complex performance, discovered the correlation and importance of relational
skills for an organization to become a strong owner organization. Nowadays, many researchers
shed light on relational capabilities from the informal relationship perspective. However, based
on (G. M. Winch, 2009), it is worth noting as a reminder that the owner organizations have to
comply with the local and international legislation as well. In addition, the relational behaviours
may differ from country to country and from region to region. Therefore, the optimal solution
to achieve the most attractive relationship between the parties requires a powerful combination
of formal and transparent trust.
The aforementioned commercial capabilities provide different values from a private to a pub-
lic organization. According to the findings of (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016), the commercial
capabilities for the construction agencies are mainly influenced by the organization’s market po-
17
==PAGE:32==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
sition and the power it holds. The success factors for the capabilities could be related to the suppli-
ers’ skills and abilities to deliver products (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016). Based on this argument,
the critical success factors for the capabilities needed for an owner organization are highly linked
with the organization’s procurement activities. In addition, public organizations operate under
the commands of the politicians; thus, their decisions significantly influence the way the owner
organization can form their commercial capabilities. Examining in more depth the capabilities
that politicians influence, it can be seen that all strategic decisions subject to strategic partnerships
and legal agreements, such as contracts, are primarily regulated (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016).
In summary, the commercial capabilities shed light on how the organization works with the
project organization, such as suppliers, vendors, and other supply firms. Based on the literature,
the commercial capabilities were depicted in the table below. In this research, the contracting
capability defined by (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) is correlated with the procurement capability.
Commercial Capabilities
Packaging
Procurement
Authority and Legal Processing
Relational
Table 2: Commercial Capabilities Based on Literature
2.2.3
Governance Capabilities
Governance capabilities are mainly those organizational competencies required in order for
a strong owner organization to interface successfully with a temporary project organization
(G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016). From another point of view, from a marketing perspective,
governance capabilities refer to the skills that an organization must possess in order to "orga-
nize, coordinate, and manage its relationship with a channel partner" (Heide, 1994; Gilliland &
Bello, 2001; Ghantous, Das, & Chameroy, 2018). These perspectives emphasize that while two
or more organizations collaborate, governance skills play a crucial role in effectively establishing
18
==PAGE:33==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
and maintaining good collaboration with the other party throughout the project life cycle. While
governance frameworks were introduced more than twenty years ago in the United Kingdom
and Norway, however, in Denmark, they are not yet firmly established (Biesenthal, Clegg, Ma-
halingam, & Sankaran, 2018). According to (Ahola, Ruuska, Artto, & Kujala, 2014) the literature
upon the project governance in the project management field is limited, even though the last
years’ many researchers are focusing more and more on that area. Also, based on (G. Winch &
Leiringer, 2016) there is not even a single investigation about benefits realization in the area of
construction project governance under the project management research.
Therefore, there is a need to conduct thorough research in the construction sector about gov-
ernance capabilities to find the necessary organizational competencies in the context of project
management research to fill that gap. In order to do so, a comprehensive review based on the
academic and industrial literature was defined and a set of governance options would be used
as a basis for building a governance framework. Initially, the governance capabilities according
to (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) were identified, examined, and reviewed in detail in order to
determine if they could be suitable for the Danish industry. Then, a set of governance capabil-
ities from other academic and industrial sources were defined and scrutinize the existing set of
capabilities defined by (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016).
Looking at (Kujala et al., 2020) research, it is important to investigate the behavior of the
parties involved in the project implementation. These parties are considered as part of an inter-
organizational project network. In tandem, it is essential to shed the light on the role of the
involved organizations in the design of the project governance from their point of view. So,
the behavior and interactions between the owner organization and the involved parties from the
inter-organizational network structure of companies perspective were identified. Considering
what kind of activities and competencies the owner assigns to the key actors can indicate what
skills are missing from the owner organization to become independent, thus, strong owner orga-
nization. Therefore, these capabilities will have the highest priority for the owner organization
to acquire in order to become a strong owner.
One of the first important governance capabilities according to (G. Winch & Leiringer,
19
==PAGE:34==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
2016) is assurance. Assurance capabilities focus on the internal activities an organization man-
ages to inform the executive management about the progress of a project (National Audit Office,
2010). As the project progresses, the organization needs to know who is responsible for making
decisions, what these decisions are about, and when these decisions were made. According to
the findings of (Biesenthal et al., 2018; Young, Young, Jordan, & O’Connor, 2012) the assurance
on the government projects lacks compliance with the assurance arrangements. Therefore, both
public and private organizations should focus more on ensuring compliance with project assur-
ance. In order to do so, a stage-gate approach could be significantly practical, especially in the
front-end phase of the project while the cost remains low. Although knowledge at the start of a
project is lower than at the final stage, this does not mean that the owner’s assurance is reduced.
Also, the owner’s role is crucial as the project progresses through detailed planning and imple-
mentation. According to (Hone, Higgins, Galloway, & Kintrea, 2011) an efficient approach in
order to secure assurance compliance consists of three phases. The first phase is related to the
internal project controls within the project team; the second phase indicates internal assurance
compliance outside of the responsibilities of the project team controlled by the program manage-
ment team; the third phase is linked with the internal audit. These three phases are considered as
three levels of assurance compliance defense. The two approaches mentioned above are consid-
ered as bureaucratic formalized procedures to secure project assurance. A significant disadvantage
of using these formal approaches is the lack of prevention. In other words, troubleshooting efforts
have already been delayed as the problem that should have been avoided has already occurred.
In addition, based on (Grenny, Maxfield, & Shimberg, 2007) there is a well-known crisis in the
industry because crucial information about the progress of the project is not discussed by the re-
sponsible. Thus, (G. M. Winch, 2009) identified the need to allocate an owner project manager
in the site to supervise the progress of the activities and participate in the critical discussions.
Moreover, another important governance capability is project coordination. Project coor-
dination capabilities focus on the organizational controls to secure the ongoing progress of the
project’s life cycle. In addition, based on (Kujala et al., 2020) project coordination is essential
to coordinate the project participants so that the delivery of the project meets the expectations.
According to (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016), there are mainly two primary data that an organi-
20
==PAGE:35==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
zation effectively uses to monitor the ongoing performance of projects. These data refer to the
project schedule and budget. In other words, project controls include the collection of sched-
ule and budget data from the implementation status and comparisons with the project execution
plan. Based on this data, a baseline can be created to monitor project progress. In that way, an
organization can quickly realize if there are delays on the project tasks based on the previous con-
trol and forecast the progress on the upcoming activities of the project. (G. Winch & Leiringer,
2016) point out a challenge that the documentation of these procedures by the project managers
does not determine who is responsible for which project coordination activities. That challenge
support the findings from (Merrow, Sonnhalter, Somanchi, & Griffith, 2009) who investigated
the performance of the project during the implementation phase based on the United Kingdom’s
construction industry. The findings of this study identified a significant difference in project per-
formance during execution between the organization that relied entirely on the project execution
plan of its contractors and the owners’ organizations that managed their project coordination ac-
tivities. The latter showed significantly higher project performance.
In addition, it is essential to consider the perspective of the key actors when it comes to
project coordination between the owner organization and the temporary organizations. In that
way, the owner organizations could enhance their competencies of what is needed to execute
projects more effectively by having a thorough understanding of the needs of the other involved
parties. To secure effective project coordination across the involved parties of the project, the work
processes need to be adopted by the parties efficiently. In order to do so, the projects’ activities
should be compatible across the actors. A traditional way of standardizing and compatibility of
work processes and tools is to state all procedures in legal agreements. However, it is worth noting
that contracts and project plans do not always cover the entire aspect of project implementation.
That is why informal types of coordination are so important. According to (Lu, Guo, Qian, He,
& Xu, 2015; van Marrewijk & Smits, 2016), informal types of coordination can have a significant
impact on stakeholder cooperation. Informal types are usually based on common values and
rules. (N. D. Caldwell, Roehrich, & Davies, 2009) argue that these rules can be stated even within
a legal agreement, where the key indicators refer to the rules and expectations for the project.
However, the main challenge with informal coordination is the tendency of people to have their
21
==PAGE:36==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
prejudices and cultural boundaries and therefore not follow the rules of equity, regardless of a
contract (Kujala et al., 2020). It is also equally important for all actors to have the information
and knowledge to complete the project successfully. This information can be disclosed either
formally, such as legal agreements, or informally such as conversations. (Brady & Davies, 2014)
propose the creation of a central model environment, where all information will be gathered
there, and all actors will have easy access. Last but not least, it is significantly vital to deal with
the unforeseen risk when the project progress is not going according to the plan because of
unexpected conditions. Whether these unexpected conditions come from external or internal
factors, there should be a formal or informal way to deal with them. In matters related to the
cooperation in-between the actors, (Kujala et al., 2020) propose to solve their problems either
under by mutual agreement or through a legal process where an independent third party takes
over the judge. Therefore, the independent third party that has the authority to act could be the
owner organization in the first place.
In addition, the perspective of the key actors in project monitoring was reviewed. Mon-
itoring ensures that all actors follow the rules and support performance-based incentives to be
applied (Kujala et al., 2020). Identifying specific, measurable, achievable, realistic, accurate goals
and performance targets is vital. In addition, monitoring enables tracking of the project perfor-
mance and relieves the stakeholders’ concerns about it. It is worth noting that the purpose of
monitoring has to do with the prevention of intentional manipulation of progress data (Reve &
Levitt, 1984). Many researchers, over time, shed light on the significant importance of contin-
uous project control and monitoring. As a starting point, the organization should create clear
and well-defined key performance indicators, where they can be used in order to measure the
performance over time. Moreover, well constructed legal project agreements can specify formal
procedures in order to keep track of the process (Lu et al., 2015; Nisar, 2013). Traditionally, the
owner organization is responsible for monitoring the work effectively and efficiently. However,
if the owners lack these capabilities, then external partners are hired to carry out these tasks on
their behalf (Reve & Levitt, 1984; Kujala et al., 2020). Therefore, a strong owner organization
should possess monitoring capabilities to effectively control and measure the key actors’ perfor-
mance over the project’s lifetime. It can be seen that monitoring the project performance over
22
==PAGE:37==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
the project’s lifetime is an essential part of project coordination. Last but not least, monitoring
was defined as a criterion or critical success factor for better project coordination.
Another important governance capability according to (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) is asset
management. Asset management addresses the capabilities needed for an organization to develop,
operate, and maintain its assets. Specifically, asset integration capabilities focus on the organiza-
tion’s activities to integrate the final asset into the existing operation. The operators and users
in the line of business are primarily working with the new assets. In this way, the responsibil-
ity for the efficient use of the assets lies with the operators. Accordingly, the operators create the
so-called owner’s operational organization, and use the asset in a way that is beneficial to the orga-
nization. (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016). One aspect of asset integration, according to (G. Winch
& Leiringer, 2016) is systems integration. Traditionally, the owner organization place an order to
the suppliers based on the elements of the system they needed. Therefore, the unique responsible
for the systems integration was the owner organization. However, (Davies & Mackenzie, 2014)
support the traditional way of systems integration is extremely difficult when it comes to "large,
one-off "systems of systems" projects." According to that, (Prencipe, Davies, & Hobday, 2003) are
as relevant as ever when they address that the systems integration emerged from the traditional
way to the new way of outsourcing. Based on the aforementioned twofold arguments, a question
arose whether a strong owner organization should in-source or out-source to integrate the final
products. Therefore, further empirical study research on existing best practices in the industry is
required to distinguish under which theme the in-house or outsourcing asset integration is most
appropriate.
The aforementioned governance capabilities provide different values from private to public
organizations. According to the findings of (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016), the construction
agencies identified a problematic situation when it comes to maintaining a strategic investment
portfolio that allows them to retain a complete set of governance competencies. In addition, they
highlighted that all the public agencies participants of their study concluded in the following
concern, that an owner organization needs to have the right set of governance capabilities to
monitor the progress of their projects. It is not always sustainable to allow their suppliers to track,
monitor, and report the project status to the owner organization. A dilemma could be whether it
23
==PAGE:38==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
is optimal for an owner organization to have its representative team on-site to ensure that progress
reports are accurate and whether suppliers will be allowed to take on this role.
In this research, the broader perspective of asset management was added as a governance
capability, correlating it to the above-reviewed asset integration capability. In addition, the iden-
tified monitoring capability became an integral part of the project coordination capability. Fur-
thermore, it can be seen that the key actors and the owner organization highly value the project
coordination capability. In the table below is depicted a summary of the governance capabilities
an owner organization requires to become strong based on (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016; Kujala
et al., 2020).
Governance Capabilities
Assurance
Project Coordination
Asset Management
Table 3: Governance Capabilities Based on Literature
In short, in the construction industry, governance capabilities require more attention from
researchers. In particular, from the point of view of the owners’ organization, it is important to de-
fine a set of governance competencies required to make an organization independent. Currently,
the management research community is not yet there to fully support the academic literature on
this topic. The focus must be both theoretical and practical. As a comprehensive literature review
on the governance capabilities have been extensively studied by the authors of the dissertation.
Therefore, it was necessary to focus on shedding light on the governance skills supported by
industry data.

to become strong.
2.3
Enabling Dimensions
This section covers the enabling dimensions part of the literature review. The enablers re-
viewed was inspired from a Ph.D. proposal done by Prof Christian Thuesen on owner capability develop-
ment at Danish railway client. The authors have chosen to review these enablers as they believe they could
contribute to further develop the OCMM model in future research. The term enabling dimensions, or
often mentioned as enablers in this report refers to the transformational capabilities needed for
owner organization, to develop capabilities relating to the strategic, commercial, and governance
dimensions. In this section, transformational capabilities, that were proven successful in other
industries such as aerospace and car industries were reviewed, as other enablers that are theoreti-
cally effective as well. This included topics as systems engineering, enterprise project organising,
leadership, and strategic partnerships.
2.3.1
Systems Engineering
Systems engineering is a field of engineering management that focuses on complex systems
in the design, integration, management of these systems and their life cycle. In this section of
the literature review, the focus was on reviewing the recent research and best practices around
systems engineering. For example, (Roehrich, 2020b) is a report and a study issued by the civil
25
==PAGE:40==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
engineering institute in the UK that mainly discusses the industry issue of project delivery. ICE
reviewed the benefits of applying systems thinking to construction delivery. It was done by con-
ducting interviews with practitioners in different industries from construction, aerospace, defense,
oil & gas, and the technology sector. The outcome of their review led to the creation of what
they called SAID (Systems approach to construction delivery) to ease delivering better projects
in the sector. The model developed by (Roehrich, 2020b) adopts best practices from the different
project-based industries, where it has been shown that the systems engineering approach works.
Figure 3: An Example of a V-cycle Diagram Taken from US Department of Transportation,
Federal Highways Administration, Office of Operations
When talking about system engineering, it is often captured in the V-cycle diagram, for
instance, the one shown in 3. According to the findings from (Roehrich, 2020b) applying systems
engineering will force engineers to work backward on a project, which means having the end
state as an objective, for example, a fully functioning airport. First, a requirement set is created for
how the system should function. Then a system architecture is set in place that will describe how
the asset will meet those requirements; eventually, this system will be broken into subsystems
to structure the project deliverable. These subsystems could be air traffic control and baggage
handling in the airport for example. Different specialists teams will design the subsystem. Then
other teams will be responsible for the construction, integration, and testing of the whole system
26
==PAGE:41==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
and how the subsystems function together. (Roehrich, 2020b) highlights the lack of systems
engineering use in the sector and emphasizes its ability to support outcomes for complex projects.
The SE approach is not a new hot topic in the industry. (Aslaksen et al., 2012) is a guide created
by INCOSE construction working group that focuses on re-positioning traditional SE practices
in the construction sector after it was successfully implemented in other industries. The guide’s
scope covers the application of SE in projects; this includes design stages in case it has not been
completed before going into the construction phase. However, their guide emphasizes mainly the
construction phase where SE could be applied. One of the key motivators addressed in (Aslaksen
et al., 2012) is the need for SE to manage risks in a very dynamic change environment that
construction projects have over their respective timescales. Moreover, due to the complexity
level, this kind of projects usually encounters, a more structured and systematic approach would
facilitate the planning and control of tasks in these projects.
In order to understand how and where SE could be applied, it is vital to understand the life
cycle of a construction project.
Figure 4: An Example of an construction Project Life-Cycle (Aslaksen, 2012)
Such projects usually consist of two main phases, a development phase where the engineering
solution is formed, and then a delivery phase where the solution is realized through procurement
of contractors, as shown in figure 4. Therefore, the primary approach used by (Aslaksen et al.,
2012) is by comparing these phases of the project life-cycle between the different industries,
which in turn helped in realizing what and where the challenges are.
27
==PAGE:42==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
2.3.2
Project Organizing
In this part of the Literature review, the state of the art of project organizing was reviewed.
As the name of the enabler defines itself, project organizing is about how the project organiza-
tional setup is functioning. Recent empirical literature focuses on improvement potentials in how
project organizations are structured in the construction phase of delivery. One of the most recent
initiatives going on in the sector is Project 13 (ICE & ICG, 2021), which is a conceptional case to
report transactions to enterprises. Working closely with ICE, ICG is currently identifying best
practices to improve the development and delivery of construction.
Figure 5: Project Enterprises Organization Derived from (ICG,ICE,2021)
The figure above 5 shows the difference between the traditional project organizational struc-
ture and the enterprise model of project organizing, which was mentioned in (ICE & ICG, 2021).
Project 13 approach argues that the transition to an enterprise-based model is fundamental for
delivering construction projects and eventually leads to better productivity. According to (ICE &
ICG, 2021) an enterprise model is a long-term agreement on a relationship between the differ-
28
==PAGE:43==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
ent project parties. These parties are the Investors, Key Suppliers, Key Advisors, Integrator, and
the Owner. These organizations are usually aligned commercially to deliver a better product to
the end-user. The primary and significant differences between the enterprise-based model the
traditional project organizing are the following:
• First, the owner plays a central role in the enterprise model, actively interacting with the
investor, key supplier, key advisor, and integrator. In contrast, in the traditional transac-
tional structure, the owner relationship is dominant with the investor and the client. The
integrator’s role in the enterprise model is to engage and integrate with the suppliers and
advisors to the working ecosystem. It can be argued that the integrator, the owner, supply
team work as one unit in the project life-cycle.
• The enterprise-based model’s reward system depends on the value from the final project
outcome.
• Cost and risk management are better managed across all the organizations involved in the
enterprise.
• (ICE & ICG, 2021) argues that a fundamental shift in leadership and governance is necessary
to successfully implement the enterprise-based model in project organizing.
In addition, either in a traditional or more modern approach of the project organization,
some enablers should be taken into account for the successful execution of the project. "Roles
and decision-making refer to giving actors the necessary information to understand the effect of
decisions on overall performance, enabling them to make an appropriate decision" (Kujala et al.,
2020). Usually, standard practices for defining roles and responsibilities in an organization are
stated in the contract. In addition, many studies show that besides the contractual legal agree-
ments, it is essential for an organization to have a strong project management structure to govern
its projects effectively as well (Nisar, 2013, 2013; Kujala et al., 2020). According to (Eriksson,
2010; Nisar, 2013), a critical factor for effective governance requires that the key actors have a
share in the decision-making process and outcome. Also, they believe that the project team should
be part of the decision-making. Based on (Nisar, 2013) one of the success factors of governance
29
==PAGE:44==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
is to emphasize the governance of the relationship between the host organization and the key
actors. According to (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016), the owner organization should have the
largest share of the decision-maker processes. On top of that, the owner organization should be
responsible for the roles and decisions during the project’s lifetime. Therefore, the owner orga-
nization can give sufficient flexibility to the key actors to manage their activities to follow the
strategy and requirements of the owner organization.
The roles, responsibilities, and behavior of which person has to act under what circumstances
according to their knowledge, area of expertise, and hierarchy will be further discussed in the next
section.
2.3.3
Leadership
Leadership as a role and activity is a significant enabling factor that the owner should possess
in administrating the delivery of constructions projects. According to (Kedri & Unsplash, 2021)
the digitization and industrialization revolution are transforming the construction industry now
more than ever, which in turn demands redefinition and a special focus on leadership, talents &
competencies, culture, and last but least organizational structures. One of the key roles industry
leaders will have a focus on is not only retaining and developing talents but also attracting new
ones by communicating the whole industry role to the society and organizational value (Kedri
& Unsplash, 2021). This could be done by emphasizing global challenges that the industry con-
tributes to solving, such as climate change, sustainable construction, diversity and equity, and
inclusion. Communicating these topics to new talents will address the value of new professionals’
impact on the industry. "Sustainable solutions are an important element of engagement for our
staff and the next generation of talent coming into the organization," said AECOM President
Lara Poloni. "This makes it a huge factor in terms of retention and in providing the talent supply
that we need to address the construction challenges." (Kedri & Unsplash, 2021)
According to a survey done by the GII summit 2021 (Kedri & Unsplash, 2021), the out-
come confirmed the need in the industry to have a solid proposition for attracting and retaining
talents, besides commencing solid visionary leadership. A critical approach to do that will be
to prioritize the skills that will define the construction industry’s future. One skill that is men-
30
==PAGE:45==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
tioned in this study is analytics, where it is believed that employees in this industry need shift
from experience-based decision making to data-driven decision making. In addition to that, ca-
pabilities and competencies in off-site production such as prefabricated raw materials used in the
execution phase of projects and biodiversity impact. Acquiring these new capabilities will enable
delivering projects quicker, safer, and more efficient. (Kedri & Unsplash, 2021) also focused on
emphasizing the importance of soft skills where; this quote was mentioned from the summit "On
the customer front, we need strong communication and collaboration skills. Employees of the
future are going to need to be able to be responsive to evolving customer needs in ways they have
not had to before," Wieland said.
Moreover, reward enablers indicate the incentives for successfully aligning project objec-
tives with stakeholders (Kujala et al., 2020). The stakeholder can be internal and/or external in
every hierarchy position. According to several studies, the motivating means could be regard-
ing monetary rewards or penalties, directly or indirectly linked with the joint performance of
the stakeholders and employees (Davies et al., 2014). In addition, rewards regarding the "con-
ditional future payments and work prompting a life cycle approach" (Kujala et al., 2020; Nisar,
2013; van den Hurk & Verhoest, 2015). In addition, they could be regarding the reputation and
brand name of the actors as well, for instance, as reputational scoring systems (Guo et al., 2014).
The rewards often are bidding on a legal agreement in-between the parties. In this way, the
type, amount, and predefined requirements about the reward program are legally stated. More
specifically, under a legal agreement such as a contract, there is a clear definition of the results
that have to be delivered within the "given time, budget, acceptable behavior" (Lu et al., 2015).
On the one hand, stating the reward system under a legal agreement, the flexibility, innovation,
and the exploitation of new opportunities are significantly reduced. On the other hand, the or-
ganization needs to address and deal with the unforeseen risks by having a contract. It is crucial
to effectively determine who is responsible for what actions and distribute the risk accordingly.
Risk-sharing can play a role in ensuring that actors align with common performance goals estab-
lished by the owner organization. However, legal agreements cannot anticipate every eventuality
and only provide limited protection; therefore, an alternative solution to shift from contractual
risk-sharing to ownership structures is highly recommended. Based on (Lu et al., 2015) rela-
31
==PAGE:46==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
tional governance is required to change actors’ behavior. Relation governance plays a vital role
in fostering a sense of ownership among the organization.
An article written by (Manyika et al., 2017) estimates that up to 375 million workers, which
represents up to 14% of the global workforce, will need to switch to different job categories by
the year 2030, and this is mainly due to the technological revolution happening in the world.
Following the pandemic, this number is estimated to increase by 11%. Leveraging opportunities
from similar industries such as aerospace to attract talents that bring transferable skills to the in-
dustry. Besides working closely with governmental institutions to establish more robust training
programs.
Furthermore, (Kedri & Unsplash, 2021) discusses the importance of embracing hybrid and
collaborative working models. This came into effect after the COVID-19 pandemic has brought
to the world, which is adjusting business as usual to fit into a hybrid working model of both
remote and on-site work. This led to a cost-benefit assessment of the future of the workspace,
as there is not so much flexibility around construction work, but as an outcome, it would make
more to keep some of the activities online.
A recent case study was done by the APM in collaboration with the IPA (Kortantamer, 2019),
aimed to provide a systemic leadership framework that can contribute to capability development,
which could be in owner organizations in regards to this research. The case study was based on
a qualitative study to get insight from project delivery professionals with significant experience
in delivering transformation projects in the governmental sector. (Kortantamer, 2019) viewed
leadership as an activity rather than focusing on the behavioral aspect of it and the different styles
that individuals possess when holding leadership positions. The main findings from their analysis
are summarised in the table below.
