These identified leadership activities from the findings presented in the table above 5 would
be very beneficial in supporting the maturity model of strong owner capabilities, as it could
benchmark the organization’s direction in the field in order to improve capability maturity.
Moreover, the analysis made by Kortantamer and his research team led to the development of
a framework that comprises four different complexity response systems, which are made up of
multiple leaders and structural elements.
33
==PAGE:48==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Figure 6: Complexity Response Developed by (Kortantamer,2019)
(Kortantamer, 2019) defines the first complexity response as ”Bridging”. Bridging is ex-
plained as the ability of leaders to respond to this type of complexity by integrating knowledge
across the different functions within the organization and industry in general by developing trad-
ing zones. The second system is ”Positioning”, which is what they defined as the ability of leader-
ship to critically respond to the different threats and opportunities concerning authority positions
in the structured organizational functions of the key persons. This is done by structuring leader-
ship roles and relationships. The third defined term is ”Legitimizing”, which is meant by having
capable leaders able to respond to different evaluations when it comes to change that is meant
to be desirable, proper, and appropriate. They deduced that this change could be delivered by
developing an opinion formation system. The fourth and last response system defined in their
framework is ”Adapting”. By adapting (Kortantamer, 2019) discusses the ability of great leaders
to respond to shifts or impacts caused by changes at the front-phase of projects and programs
34
==PAGE:49==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
by having a shared leadership strategy. Furthermore, more research that would strengthen this
established framework are presented in the case study. This includes, as mentioned before, view-
ing leadership as an activity that would ultimately enrich new sources of leadership that would
broaden the collection and types of responses. Lastly, there will always be ways to improve this
framework by continuous learning from practice leading to further development.
2.3.4
Digitalization
According to (Bang et al., 2016), the construction sector generally suffers from the lack of
ability to learn and gain experience. The industry is very project-driven, and each project has
new prerequisites and new relationships. A key issue identified is the difficulty of transferring
knowledge from one project to another. In addition, it is essential for the owner organization to
measure its performance to improve and develop. That could be done through thorough evalua-
tions and benchmarks. Specifically, the owner organization could conduct evaluations and collect
critical information in various areas such as risk, construction costs, user satisfaction, cooperation,
etc. Moreover, it is central for the owner and operator to evaluate the existing projects and as-
sets to optimize and create maximum value when launching new projects, such as adds to the
portfolio. At this point, it is essential the significance of data and digitalization. However, lack of
resources and competencies becomes a barrier to carrying out evaluation processes, as there is a
concern due to lack of knowledge about methods etc. Nevertheless, experience shows that simple
evaluations or a few key figures are better than nothing and can form an excellent foundation to
move further on. So the challenge is to get it right and get on it. The organizations that work
in a structured way with data collection and key figures, experience that it provides a powerful
tool for dialogue with both decision-makers and partners, so they can achieve increased learning
by gaining insight into their performance. For many professional builders, it is crucial to have
the skills to document the quality of the buildings and then evaluate both their own efforts and,
not least, their own results. In society’s evaluation, the quality can not be documented. It is an
open question on centrally developed systems or builders’ developed systems for evaluation will
characterize the picture in the coming years. However, it is important that it is based on the
practical experience that individual builders have already made their mark in the field and that
targeted work is done to establish methods that match the needs and competencies of builders
35
==PAGE:50==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
and which is not too resource-intensive to use. The effort must commensurate with the results,
e.g. by making visible performance, evaluating partners, or making value creation decisions and
innovation.
2.3.5
Strategic Partnerships
(Gottlieb et al., 2020) argues that strategic partnerships between owner organization and de-
livery teams would create a repetition effect that would affect project outcome in several ways,
which they call themes. In the next section, the themes were explained and reviewed. (Gottlieb
& Haugbølle, 2010) show that discussion about the repetition effects has been circulating in the
Danish construction industry since the 1960s. It has then developed into an umbrella concept that
covers several different concepts, either based on the notion that it is possible to improve produc-
tivity at the project, company, or industry level, by benefiting from economies of scale. This can
be either in the form of economies of scale or learning gains. On the one hand, economies of scale
arise when increased production capacity leads to lower unit costs. On the other hand, learning
effects are typically achieved when technical knowledge gained through business experience is
used to make organizational changes. This results in better and more efficient use of existing
resources. The figure below illustrates the repetition effect according to Pearson and Wisner.
Figure 7: The Repetition Effect According to Pearson and Wisner, 1993: 14).
(Gottlieb et al., 2020) used the model mentioned above of repetition effect between lean-
36
==PAGE:51==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
ings and volume size in order to specify different hypotheses on how partnerships can improve
this effect. Their study defined four areas where they formulated their hypothesis around. The
first area is innovation and learning. This area focuses on how the partnership model affects the
development of innovative solutions and processes and how the partnership learns from the expe-
riences gained along the way. The basic premise of the area is that strategic partnerships, unlike
traditional forms of agreement, give rise to repetitive effects resulting from learning and mutual
interaction. However, repetitions can also be limiting. For example, they could create unifica-
tion and perhaps even a fixed development path, where new innovative solutions are overlooked
instead of what one usually does.
Moreover, through repetition, knowledge can be transferred systematically from one project
to the next, and the partnership develops a knowledge platform within the area in which the
repetitions occur. This establishes an increased insight into user needs, which inspires innovation.
At the same time, long-term relationships and more significant revenue create a new, better
business basis for establishing formal innovation activities, as the individual project is no longer
sub-optimized but overall throughout the partnership’s duration.
The second area examined by (Gottlieb et al., 2020) was how the partnership model affects
the organization and collaboration, at the individual projects and portfolio level, between the key
stakeholders in and around the partnership. Repetitive interactions and collaboration between a
fixed circle of participants play a significant role in reducing the uncertainty of a project. These
uncertainties include the needs of the user, the parties’ mutual relations, and the possible technical
solutions that can be used. The assumption is that greater security around these uncertainties
enables the development of more flexible processes, efficient division of labor, and a more well-
thought-out and executable project that addresses users’ needs. At the same time, it will be easier
for a project team to set common goals and align expectations with each other through repetitions.
Through the use of relational components, the partnership thus has a greater chance of achiev-
ing an effective interaction than the traditionally defined collaboration. It is characterized by a
higher degree of synergy, complementary roles, and trust. In this perspective, partnerships will
positively impact the number of conflicts, employee satisfaction, work environment, dialogue,
communication, and user satisfaction. However, realizing these effects requires the composition
37
==PAGE:52==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
and development of relevant competencies. Moreover, as the partners commit themselves over a
more extended period, they become more dependent on each other. Therefore, it is essential that
the right competencies are present from the start and that any missing competencies and skewed
relationships are continuously handled.
The third area examined the economic and temporal conditions regarding the partnership
model. The focus here was on quantifying the effects of the partnership model in order to provide
a basis for subsequent qualitative analysis of causal relationships between the partners’ instruments
and the realized gains. Although experience with strategic partnerships demonstrates that, in the-
ory, economic and time gains can be reaped as a consequence of, among other things, reduced
transaction costs and recurring effects (certain factors complicate the image and which is exam-
ined in the evaluation done by (Gottlieb et al., 2020). Transaction costs are understood to mean
the costs associated with a customer and a provider entering into a contract and monitoring de-
liveries. Although the transaction costs of a partnership are lower than those of traditional forms
of contract, there may be significant costs associated with internal coordination (i.e., the rela-
tional components) between the parties. These costs pull in the other direction unless the parties,
through a learning process, manage to develop agility and cost-effective combination of formal
and informal coordination mechanisms that reduce the marginal cost of coordination (“The prac-
tice of contracting in public private partnerships: Transaction costs and relational contracting in
the Irish schools sector”, 2008). In addition to a possible reduction of transaction costs between
the parties in the strategic partnerships, there is also a possible broader financial / time benefit
associated with the partnership as a form of agreement. This may include financial or time gains,
which cannot be immediately calculated at the project or company level but must be considered at
the portfolio level and in a long-term perspective that extends beyond the partnership agreement
time horizon. The quantitative study by (Gottlieb et al., 2020) will examine further.
This area focused on how the partnership model affects the quality and architecture of the
projects built. This connection examines whether the partnership model has impacted the scope
and nature of repetition and innovation in task solutions. This is about the effect of the partnership
model and its expectations with a focus on flaws and shortcomings, functionality and quality,
architectural expression, and tools that lead to value creation. The study dealt with quality and
38
==PAGE:53==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
architecture about the process and product factors. In light of the discussion on the effect of the
partnership model on quality and architecture, issues concerning the repetition effect emerge as
an essential common denominator in the follow-up study. Summing up, in the table below, the
summary of the enablers is depicted.

3
Methodology
3.1
Design Science Research Methodological Approach
The methodology chosen for this thesis dissertation is the Design Science Research (DSR)
methodology. There are many similarities between (DSR) and the design research methodology
(DRM). For instance, both methodological approaches use the iterative analytical approach be-
tween descriptive and prescriptive (empirical) methods. One of the most essential characteristics
of DSR is that it uses an iterative approach in a close exchange of data and information between
the academic, industry literature, and industry practitioners (Gregor & Hevner, 2013). In ad-
dition, DSR is well-considered among the researchers as an appropriate and practical approach,
where the paradigm strives for close interaction between the relevant knowledge base and the
practical research environment (Hevner, March, Park, & Ram, 2004).
Therefore, the DSR is a handful approach when it comes to developing a framework for
strong owner capability development, which is considered a new knowledge contribution to the
construction industry. This was the most critical difference in-between the two methodological
approaches. After identifying the research questions’ problem definition, the developed method-
ological structure was created based on the DSR approach. The figure 8 illustrates the design
research methodology .
40
==PAGE:55==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Figure 8: Project methodology based on the DSR approach
3.2
Phase 0: Models
In phase 0, the models and concepts about the analysis and results were explained and further
discussed. It is worth noting that the topics discussed in this phase 0 were the necessary prelimi-
nary knowledge and information to introduce the framework of the phenomenon studied. First,
the capability map presented and then the maturity model.
3.2.1
Capability Map
Nowadays, there are many capability mapping models available. Initially, these models were
designed to align the needs of the information technology (IT) and the business. These models
focus primarily on addressing the significant challenges an organization faces when selecting
and choosing to have a set of capabilities needed to fill the gap between the complex IT system
and the organization. Even though many studies showed that organizational performance could
41
==PAGE:56==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
be significantly increased with the alignment of business and IT, remarkably little attention is
given by the executives (Luftman & Brier, 1999; Luftman & Kempaiah, 2007; Kappelman et al.,
2018; Bondel, Faber, & Matthes, 2018). One of the most widely accepted systems to increase the
performance of the organization is the so-called Enterprise Architecture Management mechanism
(Matthes, Buckl, Leitel, & Schweda, 2008).
The Architecture Management mechanism is an essential mechanism that supports the man-
agement to steer and drive the organization in the desired direction. This mechanism could be
applied to various organization’s aspects, such as the level of construction and the organization
of the business (Buckl, Matthes, & Schweda, 2010). It is worth noting that the capability map
model is the model of the Architecture Management mechanism most often used in the industry
in order to align the challenges in-between the business and the IT by the professionals (Freitag,
Matthes, Schulz, & Nowobilska, 2011). Based on that a capability map model was designed for
the construction industry in this research. It is worth bearing in mind that the focus was remained
on how an organization can map the capabilities needed to align its needs and tackle its challenges
to become a strong owner organization.
3.2.2
Structure of the Capability Model
The structure of the capability map model were presented in this section. As an input, the
business capability map requires well-defined organizational capabilities. Building on top of that,
all the capabilities can be allocated and organized using the capability map. The capability map
model is very popular among the industry specialists since it provides an architectural viewpoint
of the organization. Specifically, the capability map model gives the user the methods to visualize
a set of "business functions enabling the company’s business model and reflecting the company’s
strategy" (Freitag et al., 2011; Bondel et al., 2018). Moreover, the capabilities in a business ca-
pability map are often organized into logical categories or levels, with hierarchical connections
between business capabilities illustrated to some extent on level two to aid in the more effective
analysis and planning (Blair & Marshall, 2016). The following figure shows an example of the
capability map model. The picture illustrates five business capabilities on level one and twelve
business capabilities on level two.
42
==PAGE:57==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Figure 9: Example of the capability map model (Bondel et al., 2018)
3.2.3
Maturity Model
Today, there are many maturity models available, each tailored to a specific area and tailored
to the needs of each organization. For example, some maturity models focus on the construc-
tion industry, although the adaption is still in an early phase; thus, some of the specifications are
similar to the more traditional maturity models. Connecting the dots according to (Battista &
Schiraldi, 2013), capability maturity model refers to a conceptual model that assesses an orga-
nization’s level of maturity by considering a well-defined set of structured levels. These levels
show how effectively behaviors, methods, and processes can achieve the desired results reliably
and sustainably.
Therefore, the maturity model can be very effective in supporting an organization to un-
derstand the current level of organizational skills and support it to create the appropriate strategy
to increase its performance. On top of that, by looking at the management team of the organi-
zation, the maturity model can help them to successfully create a baseline of current capabilities
and support them to drive upcoming changes (Becker, Knackstedt, & Pöppelbuß, 2009).
43
==PAGE:58==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
3.2.4
Structure of the Maturity Model
The structure of the maturity model was addressed in this section. The Capability Matu-
rity Model (CMM) is a development model that emerged in the early 1990s due to a study of
data collected from organizations that had contractual agreements with the United States De-
partment of Defense. During all of these years, the capability maturity models were developed
for many industries such as pharmaceuticals (Paulk, 2009). In recent years, capability maturity
models from the perspective of the project management research field have become increasingly
attractive (Fengyong & Renhui, 2007). Most likely, the existing maturity models have a stan-
dardized structure where they can be easily recognized and used among researchers all around
the world (Maier, Moultrie, & Clarkson, 2011).
Inspired by the existing maturity models and to shed light on the level of organizational
competencies that an organization needs for the efficient and effective execution of projects, the
capability maturity model was created for the concept of a strong owner organization. Follow-
ing the existing maturity model approach, and inspired by (Sowden, Hinley, & Clarke, 2010;
Caracchi, Sriram, Semini, & Strandhagen, 2014; K. Liu, Su, & Zhang, 2018; Wang, Liu, Li, Luo,
& Liu, 2020), the maturity model based on the research questions and objectives of this research
was created. The figure 7 depicts the overview of the maturity models used in order to create the
strong owner maturity model (OCMM).

Influenced by the maturity models mentioned above models in the construction, ship design,
and project management industry, the following key elements for the model’s design are taken
into account. The key elements include the Maturity level, Dimensions, capabilities, Cataloguing,
Critical Success Factors - Assessment Criteria, and Framework matrices, which were further
described in the next section.
• Maturity level
Maturity model of this research consists of 5 different maturity levels. These levels evolve
with a step-by-step approach, with the lowest level being Level 1 to the highest at Level 5.
These levels are linked with the performance of the organizational capability.
• Dimensions
45
==PAGE:60==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Dimensions are on the high level the categories of the capabilities. They refer to the three
aspects of the organizational level in business. These dimensions refer to the strategic, com-
mercial, and governance.
• Capabilities
Capabilities refer to organization competencies an organization needs to significantly in-
crease its performance at project execution. A list of capabilities is listed from the spectrum
of a public and private organization. These capabilities are the central focus of this model.
• Cataloguing
Cataloging is included in the maturity model to create and maintain a unique id for every
capability and criterion. It can be seen in the form of an alphabetic letter next to a number.
• Critical Success Factors - Assessment Criteria
Evaluation criteria refer to the critical success factors, where a capability is divided into a set
of smaller capability-factors. It is worth noting that the organization should simple focus
on maturing the critical success factors so that the dependent capability matures simultane-
ously.
• Framework matrices
Success factor criteria matrix describes in detail the full definition for every success factor
criterion at five maturity Levels. Also, the Capabilities Development matrices depicts all the
capabilities needed for an organization to increase its organizational performance at project
execution. In addition, in the latter tables are included the different academic and industrial
literature justification which shows the importance of these capabilities. The tables can be
found in the attached excel file.

==PAGE:61==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
3.3
Phase 1: Literature Review
This dissertation was scoped around two research questions stated in the thesis objectives in
section 1.3.
Research Question 1: What are the capabilities required by owner organizations in the construc-
tion industry to execute projects more effectively and efficiently?
Research Question 2: How can owner organizations in the construction industry assess their
capability performance in order to improve their performance?
The first goal was to systematically describe the characteristics of the strong owner concept
by making a capability map of strong owner capabilities on the fundamentals of the framework
developed by (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) and (G. Winch & Cha, 2020), who grouped these ca-
pabilities in 3 main dimensions, strategic, commercial, and governance capabilities. This objective
focused on the first research question posed in this dissertation.
The second goal was to develop a capability maturity model that owner organizations in
the construction industry can use. This model could help them assess their current capabilities
maturity. Then, based on the assessment, draw a road map for further development to improve
capability performance. This goal was the research domain used to answer the second research
question in this research.
The first essential part of this thesis was conducting a literature review to familiarize the
authors with past and recent articles, papers, and findings from the academic and industrial liter-
ature. The academic research for relevant work done in this field was done using three primary
sources: DTU Findit, Scopus and Google Scholar. In these platforms, credible references can be
found to be used for the literature review. The main search strings used in these abstract and
citation databases include keywords such as owner organizations, strong owner organizations,
construction delivery, capability development, capability map, maturity models, project man-
agement, systems engineering, project organizing, leadership, strategic partnerships. All the rest
of the keywords used are directly or indirectly related to those mentioned above. In addition,
47
==PAGE:62==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
industry reports from well-known multinationals and reputable companies in the construction,
infrastructure, or consulting industry were considered to support the industrial literature back-
ground. In addition, different areas of research were categorized during this research.
• Category 1 - Strong Owner Concept: In this category, reports, articles, and papers cover-
ing the topic of strong owner organization were gathered. These articles mainly described
the concept of a strong owner organization, which informed the authors about the back-
ground of the topic and the concept. As a result, the strong owner organization’s meaning
became clear, and the industry problem was realized. In general, the organizations from
the construction industry compared to the other industries when delivering projects on
time and within budget. One of the main papers this thesis was based on is (G. Winch &
Leiringer, 2016). In connection to that, the authors understood the value of developing a
conceptional framework of strong owner capabilities. Inspired by many researchers among
the community, the authors chose to review and build upon (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016)
and (G. Winch & Cha, 2020) research for strong owner capabilities development. There-
fore, in this dissertation, the primary categories of the capabilities development lie in the
following three dimensions: strategic, commercial, and governance.
• Category 2 - Strong Owner Capabilities: Reviewing (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) and
(G. Winch & Cha, 2020) was used to build further on their conceptional framework and
make a new version of their model. In order to do so, references were gathered to explore
the three dimensions of the framework strategic, commercial, and governance capabilities,
covering materials about all the capabilities included in that conceptional framework seen
in the table 4. As mentioned, the conceptional model was reviewed, and in order to do
so, a strategy was followed. The first step was to validate the existing capabilities from
(G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) model by making cross-references with others researchers.
The second step was to collect new capabilities from other researchers and place them under
the most relevant dimension. The reason behind that was to re-frame the (G. Winch &
Leiringer, 2016)’s model according to the latest research on the topic based on the academic
and industrial research community.
48
==PAGE:63==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
• Category 3 - Transformational Capabilities: in this category, capabilities that can transform
and develop the capabilities in category two were researched. The idea was inspired from
(Gulino, Sergeeva, & Winch, 2020) where they used the conceptional framework developed
by (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) and added a fourth dimension to it, which they named
transformational capabilities. Transformational capabilities were named enablers to be used
by owner organizations to develop owner capabilities. While researching this category, the
focus was mainly on systems engineering, project organizing, leadership, digitalization, and
strategic partnerships.
• Category 4 - Models: this category of the research entailed academic and theoretical tools
about the topic, which set the base for the analysis methodology. In addition, the models
topic covered in phase 0 such as the capability map and capability maturity model were
reviewed to use in the analysis and framework development.
All the gathered literature was managed by using the software Mendeley. "Mendeley is
a free reference manager that can help writers store, organize, note, share, and cite ref-
erences and research data." Also, "it is most known for its reference manager, which was
used to manage and share research papers and generate bibliographies for scholarly articles"
(Paul Foeckler, n.d.). After going through all the literature collected, an excel catalog was
created to sort the capabilities based on the following aspects. The aspects were based on the
author name, published year, title, category 1 (capabilities, strong owner concept, enablers),
category 2 (dimensions, enablers), document type, publication setting (academic, industry,
or mixed), knowledge type (empirical, theoretical, mixed), purpose, key arguments, con-
text, methodology (quantitative, qualitative), key findings, key quotes, notes, relevant to the
thesis. This helped in writing the literature review since it was easy to navigate between
the different categories of literature to find main arguments and systematically build up the
review. The reference catalog can be found in appendix 12.

Phase 2: First Design Phase - Iteration 1
Building upon the literature review results that were completed in phase 1, a first version of
the strong owner capability map was performed by summarising all the owner capabilities cat-
egorized in the three dimensions: strategic, commercial, and governance. In addition to that, a
prototype or generic SO maturity model was developed. The approach used when developing
the capability map’s first design, and the maturity model was based on deductive research analy-
sis. Data was gathered from many different existing literature sources to build the models. The
capability map can be seen in table 4, and the justification can be found in appendix 13. Also, the
maturity model of iteration 1 can be found in appendix 13.
The methodology and tool used to mapping out the capabilities was described and explained
in section 3.2.1. The capability dimensions were gathered and defined through the literature
review. In addition to that during the design of the first iteration of the model, the conceptual
framework presented by (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) was the focal referencing point for the
development of this model. The main capabilities described in this conceptual model, were ex-
tracted, reviewed, and challenged using other collected literature. The summary of the capability
maps for each dimension can be found in table 4. Furthermore, the transformational dimensions,
based the review of the enabling dimensions literature review together with the paper written
by (Gottlieb et al., 2020) about strategic partnerships. The summary of the enabling capabilities
maps can be found in table 6.
Moreover, the five levels of maturity were defined for each CSF under each capability. With
that in mind, criteria from different maturity models found in the literature were gathered, from
which critical success factors were extracted for each capability under each dimension. The pur-
pose for doing this was to make a first generic version of the maturity model, from which in-
terview questions were designed in a deductive reasoning approach. The models used to extract
these maturity criteria are stated in table 7. Finally, with all the criteria labeled and grouped under
each capability dimension, the questions have been built up using OCMM first iteration found in
appendix 13 to cover all the clustered and scattered criteria, or the so-called critical success factors.

3.5
Phase 3: Evaluation based on Primary Data
The methodological approach for data collection and analysis used in this research was mainly
a qualitative approach with a small focus on quantitative data. The qualitative analytical approach
chosen in this research was in the form of interviews with industry practitioners (Raw Data) and
a survey sent out to them. Moreover, the qualitative analysis approach uses subjective judgment
to analyze a value or prospects based on non-quantifiable information, such as expertise, industry
cycles, the strength of research development (Smith, 2021) which is what was needed to address
the research questions.
In this section it is described and explained the methodology behind the tools used for data
collection, the objectives behind the interview questions, and the sent-out survey. In the following
section in phase 4, the data analysis methodology was discussed in more detail.
3.5.1
Stakeholder Map
For the purpose of gathering the raw data, a stakeholder map was developed. Stakeholder
mapping is a crucial tool usually used to map out relevant stakeholders who could provide ex-
pertise, experience, or influence about a specific topic. The conventional stakeholder mapping
approach was not used in this thesis, where project stakeholders are defined on a two-dimensional
matrix according to their power and influence in the project. The limitation of the conventional
stakeholder management mapping focuses on specific stakeholders. However, this research was
focused on the industry, where the research outcome would benefit all the owner organizations
in the construction industry in Denmark. Thus, the methodology used for mapping out relevant
organizations to participate was mainly dependent on the nature of the organization. By nature
of the organization, firstly, it is meant organizations either in the public or private sector of the
construction industry. Secondly, organizations from other industries such as pharmaceuticals and
manufacturing industry, where best practices and transformable skills were of focus. These or-
ganizations have in common that they primarily act as owner, client, or consultant firms located
in Denmark. The organizations chosen should primarily operate in Denmark, but this does not
necessarily mean that they cannot operate abroad to some extent. It is worth noting that the
51
==PAGE:66==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
responsibility lies under the organizations’ side in order for them to decide whether they want
to contribute to this research. The researchers did not request sensitive data; therefore, there
was no legally binding agreement between the authors and the organizations. In addition, the
stakeholder map was created based on the following steps.
First step, mainly the network of organizations provided by Prof. Christian Thuesen’s the
thesis supervisor was used. Second step, the authors’ reached their network to contact other
organizations. The stakeholder map can be found in table 8.

3.5.2
Primary Data
The primary data consisted of semi-structured interviews’ organized with industry practi-
tioners from the stakeholder map and the survey. The second type of data which could be used is
secondary data, which would be extracted from another round of interviews with company rep-
resentatives from specific domains. As this study’s main objective was to develop a generic strong
owner capability map and maturity model, only primary was used in the analysis methodology.
To conclude, the formed research questions clearly stated what data was needed for the analysis.
Firstly, data to review (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) conceptional framework was gathered. Sec-
ondly, data to develop a maturity model for capability assessment was gathered. In order to do so,
52
==PAGE:67==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
the interview questions focused on reviewing the existing capabilities and critical success factors
defined for capabilities presented in the capability map done in phase 2. A series of predetermined
but open-ended questions were defined and presented in a guide format for informants.
The guide was structured in the following manner.
1. The first part of the guide included a brief definition of the project and problem statement.
Moreover, this part included an introduction to the interview structure where significant
and key terms were defined to the informants (strategic, commercial, and governance ca-
pabilities).
2. The second part of the guide included general and introductory questions asked to the
informants, such as introducing themselves and presenting their industry experience. This
part also included open questions about capabilities and project success used to break the ice
and introduce the informant to the next part.
3. The last part of the guide was divided into three sections, one section for each capability
dimension. Each section included questions about the capabilities under each dimension,
challenges they face in the respective organization, and their understanding of successful
project delivery in all its strategic, commercial, and governance aspects.
The full guide can be found in Appendix 10
The other form used to collect primary data was sending out a survey to the interviewed
informants, colleagues, and other industry practitioners. However, the authors ended up not
using the survey data, as it did not include the all the capabilities of the second iteration. This is
explicitly discussed in phase 5 section, and the discussion section of the report. The survey was
divided into four different sections.
1. The first section was used to get general information about the respondents’ background,
including their name, their organization, which industry they come from, and what role
they have at their organization.
2. The second section was intended to gather data regarding the respondent’s understanding
53
==PAGE:68==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
and perspective of the current situation at their organization about project execution capa-
bilities. Questions on projects performance were asked regarding quality, cost, time, and
risk. These questions were quantifiable in a percentage format. The purpose behind this
was to get an overall picture of the industry status quo in project performance.
3. The third and fourth sections were dedicated to organizational capabilities and their CSF.
In this section, the respondents were asked to prioritize the capabilities and CSF under
each of the capability dimensions. By doing this, the aim was to get data on how to rank
the capabilities and their respective CSF in the developed capability map and the maturity
model. Moreover, respondents were asked to evaluate the maturity levels of the capabilities
based on a provided 5-level defined maturity matrix.
The full survey can be found in Appendix 11
3.6
Phase 4: Second Design Phase - Iteration 2
In Phase 4, the analysis’ methodology for the gathered data from practitioners and specialists
from the industry was addressed. As mentioned, the basic principles for qualitative data analyses
were considered to make full use of the value of the data. One of the key concepts of qualitative
data analysis is that it must be methodical and adhere to the qualitative content analysis technique.
(Mayring, 2004).
By definition, the qualitative analysis data could have the form of language, figures, obser-
vations, and textual analysis. In this research study, it was decided to work on the content and
thematic analysis to retrieve the maximum value from the analysis. Content analysis refers to
classifying and debating the meanings of words, phrases, and sentences. Thematic analysis is the
process of coding and thoroughly scrutinizing data to uncover significant themes and patterns.
The data analysis started by transcribing the audio information of the informants to written
text by using Microsoft teams transcription feature in real-time while conducting the interviews.
Then corrections and adjustments were made to the transcription by thoroughly re-watching
the recorded tape of the video. Finally, to capture the whole meaning of the informant’s context,
the tone and the emotional context were probed. The steps of this data analysis can be see in the
54
==PAGE:69==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
table 10. The methodology analysis for the interview data is presented in the next section.
3.6.1
Analysis Methodology
As a first step for the analysis, content analysis was used to validate the existing capabilities in
comparison with the capabilities gathered from the empirical data. In order to do so, a tailored
tool was designed where the parameters in table 3.6.1 were in place to compare the existing with
the new capabilities— the tool was used individually for each interview. Specifically, the data
from the interviews was efficiently validated by creating an excel table and giving their values
with colors depending on those parameters. Four different parameters were defined, and each
parameter got assigned by a unique color. The parameters lie under two different categories.
Category 1 included the capabilities gathered from the interviews and their existence, or lack of
confirmation by the informants was made. In addition, there were few capabilities in the yellow
area where the informants were unsure if these capabilities existed or not. In category 2, all the
new capabilities defined by the informants were added to the model. As a result, it was efficiently
validated if respondents added new capabilities, validated existing ones, or discarded existing ones.
The tables can be found in the attached excel file.
Parameters
Confirmed - Green
Not confirmed - Red
Not sure - Yellow
Added - Blue
Table 9: Parameters
In addition, to significantly increase the effectiveness of the qualitative analysis, well-detailed
thematic analysis was performed; the interviews had already been transcribed, and the data coding
was done. The following section described the data coding process. In order to do so, the software
tool and the methodology used in that software were described.

==PAGE:70==

3.6.2
Methodology in Atlas.ti
For this study, a well-known quality data analysis software was employed to expedite and
ease the coding of the obtained data. Atlas.ti proved to be a handy tool for gaining a solid overview
and flexibility working with the data at hand. Atlas.ti was primarily used in order to analyze the
collected data from the practitioners and professionals from the industry. It is worth noting that
the network view (feature in Atlas.ti) created in connection to the coding performed on the data
gave an overview to help with the data analysis process. Summing up, the category development
methodology process of qualitative analysis used in the Atlas.ti were presented to give the reader
an understanding of the methods used. The category development process indicated what codes
were utilized and which quotes from the data were connected with them.
There are many ways of forming categories in qualitative data analysis, but in this research
study, the focus was only on the main two methods, deductive and inductive. Therefore, the
definition of deductive coding was explained first, and then inductive coding was explained af-
ter.The development of a deductive method focuses on the definition of codes based mainly on
the theoretical framework used and the research topic addressed through data collection. The
codes were set independently of the data and, before the authors became familiar with the data.
In addition, an inductive method was a continuous process that involved sieving the acquired data
and finding meanings and patterns.
As a result, the codes were then generated as required (Swain, 2018). The methodology’s
steps used to analyze the data collected from the interviews in an interpretive way can be seen in
the table below. The next section described in detail the methodology used in Atlas.ti.
56
==PAGE:71==

the research before describing the coding used to analyze the data and reach out to the outcome.
Furthermore, it is essential to note that the dimensions (Strategic, Commercial, Governance) were
defined based on the literature review, and they were fixed during the entire building process of
the capability map and the maturity model.
3.6.3
Deductive and Inductive Coding Methodology
This section described in detail how the deductive and inductive codes were generated.
Firstly, the deductive codes were the capabilities and success factors criteria (CSFs) researched
based on the literature review from Phase 1. Having the existing capabilities and critical success
factors as codes for the deductive approach seemed promising. Secondly, the inductive coding
approach was held to support finding undiscovered or new capabilities. The deductive codes used
can be found in appendix 15.
The inductive codes were derived from the transcription’s facts, arguments, and examples in
the Atlas.ti environment as an open or posterior codes. In that way, patterns were observed from
the transcriptions when undergoing the data. In addition, all information correlated directly
or indirectly with the dimensions, capabilities, critical success factors, and maturity level were
utilized to create inductive codes for further analysis and interpretation. The inductive codes can
57
==PAGE:72==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
be found in appendix 15.
In order to optimize the analysis process, the codes were organized into group families inside
the Atlas.ti. The group families dimension can be found in the appendix 28. In addition, memos
were created to support the authors keeping notes about the relevant data gathered from Atlas.ti.
Overall, the methodology for data analysis used a combination of deductive and inductive
coding. In that way, the qualitative data analysis method was categorized as a hybrid (Swain,
2018). Both deductive and inductive coding were used at various analysis phases to analyze data.
On the one hand, deductive coding was used to get information for the capability map result
extracted mainly from the data collected from the literature and interviews. On the other hand,
inductive coding extracted data directly from the interviews to validate, verify, and explore the
data for the maturity model development.
3.6.4
Interpretation to the Framework Methodology
This section explains the methodology for how the model was filled with information. The
first step to understanding the different maturity levels of the organization was to have an overview
of how many different levels exist and what information should be included in each level of the
OCMM. In order to do so, the common patterns and trends needed to be investigated to find the
borders or limits where each level would be different from the other level.
The systematic approach was based on two actions. Firstly, all the codes were connected
with specific quotations from the interviews. In that way, the context of the interviews was saved
in the best manner since the valuable information was highlighted and linked successfully with
the codes. Secondly, every dimension’s quotation was gathered and depicted together by the
handful use of the network view of the Atlas.ti. Therefore, all the insightful information from
the interviews was captured, saved, and analyzed systematically. All the quotations connected
with these codes were depicted using the codes as referring points in the network view function,
as seen in the figure 10.
As mentioned, the interviews were set up to give information on every dimension’s clustered
capabilities, critical success factors, and organizational challenges. As a result, the analysis was
58
==PAGE:73==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
feasible since most of the clustering happened for capabilities, critical success factors, and orga-
nizational challenges from different levels of the same dimension. Moreover, the different coded
quotations answered whether capability or critical success factors were met entirely in the specific
dimension, to some extent, or not at all. In addition to that, the coded quotations were used to
capture challenges that occurred in different organizations. In that way, the border or limits of
each maturity level was defined. Figure 10 shows the network view used in Atlas.ti to identify
all the quotations expressed by the industry specialists under the capital raising capability.
Figure 10: Network view of a strategic capability
It can be seen that the strategic capability depicted in the figure is the capital raising, where
the S1.1 Strategic Planning and S1.2 Political Negotiation are the critical success factors to this
capability. It is worth noting that having this visual representation can be effective and efficient
in recognizing what quotation was linked with every capability or critical success factor.
In conclusion the deductive and inductive analysis made in Atlas.ti significantly supported the
59
==PAGE:74==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
authors in the following ways. First, it helped the authors to validate the existing capabilities, find
new capabilities, and group existing and new capabilities into the most appropriate dimension.
Second, it supported the authors in finding new critical success factors under the capabilities and
validating existing ones. Third, it supported the authors in determining the maturity levels for
each capability in the best way. Overall, the results of these analyses paved the way for building
up the capability map and the maturity model.

Reflections
After analyzing the data gathered from the interviews, the second iteration of the capability
map and the maturity model was made based on the analysis outcome. As for the capability map,

valuable input from data collected.
The second iteration of the maturity model was built on excel. The excel model was divided
into several sheets. First, a summary sheet providing a holistic overview of the maturity assess-
ment, and then a detailed sheet providing a more detailed scoring in each dimension. In addition
to that, the model included one separate sheet for each capability under each dimension, where
users could assess themselves given the information provided. Moreover, in case future researchers
were to apply the survey, the data extracted from it could help in weighing the criteria within
the model; however, the criteria included in the final version of the model were not known when
the survey was sent to the organizations. For that reason, the criteria per each capability were
weighed equally.

4
Findings from the Primary Data
In this section of the report, the findings from the literature review (Phase one) and industry
interviews (Phase three) were presented and discussed. The findings in this section were done
in accordance with the research questions and the defined literature frame in this research. As
the core of the purpose statement for this research is related to exploring strong owner capabili-
ties, their relative CSF and then building a maturity assessment tool for strong owner capability
development. Therefore, the results were presented per capability, in accordance with the struc-
ture of the OCMM capability dimensions. It is worth noting that there is a dedicated section for
each of the three main dimensions: strategic, commercial, and governance. Under each dimen-
sion section, the capabilities, CSFs, challenges, bottlenecks, and maturity elements found were
elaborated. As a result, the findings presented were utilized to define each capability’s maturity
elements and maturity levels definitions to develop the second iteration of the OCMM framework
found in appendix 17, which will be discussed in section 5 of the report.
4.1
Strategic Dimension
In the methodological approach of this thesis, the structural definition of owner capabili-
ties used by (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) was adapted. The first dimension to be discussed is
the strategic dimension. Strategic capabilities are defined, controlled, and owned by the owner
organization (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016). These capabilities are activities that are the full re-
sponsibility of the owner organization as they relate to the business and corporate strategy of
the organization. Only the owner organization alone can relate the investment project benefits
to the upper strategy. The findings from our primary data included the following capabilities as
strategic capabilities, which an owner organization must develop to become a SO organization.
1. Capital Raising
2. Portfolio Management
3. Stakeholder Management
62
==PAGE:77==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
4. Environmental, Social and Corporate Governance
5. Knowledge Management
4.1.1
Capital Raising
As defined in the literature review, the term capital raising depicts an organization’s ability
to raise the capital to fund its investment projects (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016). This capability
is unquestionably an essential owner capability. The owners who lack capital raising skills cannot
raise sufficient economic sources to invest, leading to unsustainable projects. In connection to
that, if there are not enough funds to support the investment, then the project is most likely to
fail (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016). Therefore, it is clear that capital raising is a crucial strategic
capability that an owner organization must have, whether it is in a private or a public organization.
According to the analyzed data, there are mainly two drivers for successful capital raising. These
CSFs are strategic planning and political negotiation. First, strategic planning is a process in which
organizational leaders determine their vision for the future and identify their goals and objectives
for the organization. This may include planning the project pipeline of an organization. One
of the first significant findings was that organizations in the construction industry have difficulty
planning their needs for the future. This was a significant challenge, according to a Director at
the Danish Owners Association where he stated the following:
”It is very different from various types of client (owner) organizations. For instance, if it is Novo
Nordisk building a factory in Kalundborg, it is driven by the medicine market and so on, which
translates into some major business decisions. Moreover, I think it is hard for a client organization to say
we need to know 10-year pipeline in order to do what is needed to be done. It is not how it works, but
on the other hand, there are some things that are easier to plan. For instance, the maintenance and repair
are quite easy to make a prognosis for. ”
Director - Danish Owners Association
Long-term strategic planning helps owner organizations shape and better define their strate-
gic direction. Thus, the owner must be capable of making short-term planning for projects and
doing long-term strategic planning. Several industry practitioners emphasized the issue of inca-
pability of long-term strategic planning in the interviews held.
63
==PAGE:78==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Another challenge found in the industry is that many owner organizations, struggles in
defining their needed annual capital volume. This is due to the fact that there are different types
of projects that an owner organization could undertake or start, which vary from newly built
projects to renovation, refurbishment, operations management, maintenance and so on. Most
owner organizations, especially in the public sector, have difficulty making prognoses on what
type of projects they will be undertaking in the far future. Due to the diversity of project types,
annual capital volumes are not consistent from one year to the other, making it challenging for
the owner organization to plan projects on a five or ten years horizon.
”I guess one of the challenges at least is the diversity of projects at a typical member organization where
would vary from very small renovation refurbishment projects changing the usage of run one room to
another up to a project for billions of DKK, as a wide diversity in what they actually undertake in terms
of different projects. Adding to this is that the volume of a project activity also varies over time very
much so in one year, they will have a volume of maybe just 100 million and next year, they will have
an 800 million of project volume, and it is tough to build a professional organization that sort of can do
all the things that you want to do if you do not have a sort of a time horizon of more than than one or
two years. ”
Director - Danish Owners Association
Moreover, moving funds from one calendar year to another is not as simple as it may seem.
It is no secret that project delays are very common in many construction projects, which may
lead to some deviations of cost expenditure in the projects. Very often it occurs that some project
gets paused, or the whole project portfolio gets re-prioritized, which leads to cost overruns or less
money being spent, and in both cases, the situation must be explained, emphasized an industrial
Ph.D. student at Building Construction Client.
64
==PAGE:79==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”In construction, it is quite often that you have delays, and when early on during the spring we have to
make a budget for the National Audit we have to declare how much money we will spend during this
year. Then if one project gets delayed or another gets speaks up, then we actually need to have a good
explanation of why the money isn’t spent that year, or if you need to spend more money or you need to
spend less money, you have to explain both. Of course, that might be difficult, and it is in general
problem, I guess for most times I have worked for private organizations, you would also need to be able
to explain why the money is not being spent and why you’re spending more money than anticipated.
Even though the project might not cost more, it can be difficult to move money across calendar years or
budget years. ”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
In the public sector nowadays, raising funding to support investments in constructions up-
grades is significantly more structured than before. In order to provide new sources of funding
for essential projects, governments have turned to a public-private partnership. In addition to
that, it was found that the government fully finances most owner organizations, especially in the
public sector. Therefore their projects are accounted for in the annual governmental budget. So,
it is basically a matter of political direction that decides construction project capital volume.
”Well, we’re 100% publicly financed, so that’s really a political question. So political factors are
definitely impacting our portfolio.”
Section Manager A- Danish Railway Client
Some publicly financed owner organization, unlike others, has established a political influ-
ence so that politicians do not fully decide their project portfolio, but they are instead included
in the decision process and actively participate in suggesting new project ideas. Section Manager
B implicated this in the following quotation.
65
==PAGE:80==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”Of course we have some control over that because sometimes we are the ones who have a good idea of
something and come to the politician ask for it. Sometimes they agree and give us money to do a project
but in the end, they are the ones giving us the money for a project. Other times, like Aarhus-Silkeborg, it
is a political wish to do something, and they are coming up with the project. So this is one way we could
do projects.”
Section Manager B - Danish Railway Client
One last finding in the capital raising capability indicated that the private owner organiza-
tions could raise more capital for their investment projects from the public if they could contribute
to the environmental and social sustainability goals, where projects could contribute at the lo-
cal or national level. This was found to be an excellent incentive for a recognized international
manufacturing company, where a people manager said the following.
”The Norwegian Government figured out that this initiative would reduce the CO2 emissions of the
entire town by 80%. So, they approved and supported this project. We prepared the project budget and
presented our business model to them. Long story short, the Norwegian Government supported €10
million in the project out of €45 million. So the rest came from our own capital, but 10 million from
this Norwegian agency supporting sustainability projects was a big incentive.”
People Manager - International Manufacturing Company

4.1.2
Stakeholder Management
According to the reviewed literature, stakeholder management is the process of maintaining
and managing the relationships with all the parties that have any influence or impact on the
project. Managing stakeholders is identified as a vital owner capability. (G. Winch & Leiringer,
2016) define stakeholder management as the key to project management and that it is the owner’s
responsibility to handle it effectively. In addition, according to the findings of (Gottlieb et al.,
2020), they have found that partnerships indicate that this capability is vital on the part of the
owner.
Based on the analyzed data from both the literature and the industry interviews, three main
CSFs for this important owner capability were identified. The first one is decision-making.
Decision-making is the process of making choices by identifying a decision, gathering informa-
tion, and assessing alternative solutions. The second one is communication, which helps exchange
information and share leanings between the parties. Communication includes “Information ex-
change,” “Sharing learning and innovation,” and “Cost data transparency.” Finally, the third CSF
is about the owner being user-oriented and involving the end-users in decision-making through
communication and consultation processes.
One of the first findings regarding stakeholder management was that the owner needs to
ensure a transparent communication process and see project requirements from different perspec-
tives of all the stakeholders involved. According to a program manager at International Pharma-
ceutical Company, this is significantly important.
” It is again about actually communicating the value to the stakeholders, being flexible, meeting the
stakeholders’ demands, understanding where they are coming from, and understanding that we need
compliance. So I believe this is a good capability.”
Program Manager - International Pharmaceutical Company
One of the main challenges found, is that some owner organizations struggles to provide
reliable and accurate project information to the stakeholders. One of the mentioned examples
by section manager B at Danish railway client was regarding project scheduling, where such
67
==PAGE:82==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
information has a great impact on the operations of the other stakeholders involved in the project.
On the other hand, there is also the politicians who always need high knowledge about project
cost expenditure.
”They need to have reliable information at a very early stage, and the other one is at the politicians and
the people supporting the politicians in the departments of the ministries need to have very high
knowledge about how we are running the finances.”
Section Manager B - Danish Railway Client
In addition to that, it was found that it is extremely important for the owner organization to
try and maintain a good balance between the benefits and costs each decision has on the project
stakeholders. This is considered the main bottleneck in construction projects as it is tough to
satisfy all stakeholders on the same level. This finding was well emphasized by Industrial Ph.D.
at Building Construction Client.
”Every time you make a decision, you will influence the balance between the benefits and the threats to
these stakeholders individually, and you will need to be able to keep that balance during the project,
otherwise, you will end up with a project that is not a success for all parties. It might still be a success for
one party, but as I said before, I would deem that as a short term success for us.”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
Moreover, it was found that the primary and most effective ways of communication are
important for holding accountability and transparency in the industry in general. Therefore,
most owner organizations need to map a stakeholder landscape with a communication plan from
an early stage of the project, where responsibilities are defined and the project deliverables are
assigned.
68
==PAGE:83==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”On a high level, it is described how we work together, what part of the project we take care of, who is
responsible for, and what part of the project they are dealing with. But of course, as in the old project,
there is much interference between those responsibilities. So, in the planning phase, we also try to make a
stakeholder landscape and a communication plan to secure that we get the right people on board at the
right time.”
Senior Manager - Danish Municipality
Last but not least, the importance of creating a unique culture of trust between all the project
stakeholders, was found to be a major driver for successful stakeholder management, which would
optimally lead to successful project delivery in the industry.
”It is very important for us having a very close relationship with our stakeholders, and we are talking
about a lot of involvement. That means large trust, and openness. It is actually pulling them close to us.”
Senior Manager B - Danish Regional Client

4.1.3
Portfolio Management
Based on the literature review, project portfolios are projects sponsored by the same organi-
zation which shares scarce resources but does not have any technical dependencies (G. M. Winch,
2009). According to (Bower, 1972) the primary resource is the financial capital, which means that
project portfolio management is correlated with capital budgeting. In addition, an organization’s
long-term strategy, mission, and vision are embedded in the organization’s project portfolio.
69
==PAGE:84==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
The literature review gathered and analysis of the interviews led to the following findings
regarding this strategic capability. First, project selection is an important CSF in managing a
successful portfolio, which is about selecting the most beneficial projects to be included in the
portfolio. The second CSF defines a clear mission of the project, which indicates the goals, pur-
pose, and objectives of the project. Lastly, the owner organization needs to have a strategic role
in the early decisions of the projects to create better value for users.
A major challenge found in the industry in general, was the complexity behind defining
the proper requirements of the projects, from the idea or initiation phase to the construction or
execution phase of the project. In addition, the challenge of designing a constructible building
by matching the design and construction requirement was found to be a challenge according to
Industrial Ph.D. at Building Construction client.
”One of the major challenges of construction in general is the complexity of our projects. So basically
when from the offset of a construction project, one of the main capabilities needed is the skill-set to define
the proper requirements and define them enough that you will be able to design a building. Living up to
these requirements actually builds or erects the building living up to the requirements, so that thread goes
from setting the proper requirements that are design-able and then execution-able. That is probably the
main capability of success. ”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
Another main finding was the emphasis on the importance of defining the scope of the
projects in the portfolio and having the ability to plan and select projects driving the owner
organization’s strategic direction.
70
==PAGE:85==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”We will need to be able to allow ourselves to define the scope of these projects and manage our advisors.
In a way, the projects that will end up tendering out are contractible and can meet the quality and
financial goals of the project. ”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
”It is very different from various types of client (owner) organizations. For instance, if it is Novo
Nordisk building a factory in Kalundborg, it is driven by the medicine market and so on, which
translates into some major business decisions. Moreover, I think it is hard for a client organization to say
we need to know 10-year pipeline in order to do what is needed to be done. It is not how it works, but
on the other hand, there are some things that are easier to plan. For instance, the maintenance and repair
are quite easy to make a prognosis for.”
Director - Danish Owners Association
In some governmental agencies, it was found that owner organizations have very little in-
fluence on what projects they should be undertaking on a portfolio level. This happens because
it is mainly the political direction of the local municipality, region, or country that defines the
strategic construction projects to be prioritized or executed. This point was very well explained
by Industrial PhD. at Building Construction Client.
”We do not choose our own projects at all. So actually there, it does not mean that much, and it would be
the same for most other governmental agencies. For instance, if you ask the danish road directorate
whether they want to build the road or not, it does not matter because they cannot choose. It is a political
decision that a highway is to be built going from this area to this area. They might have an influence on
exactly whether the road turns a bit here or bit there. However, most of the project or the overall
framework is a political decision, and it is the same for most of our projects. ”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
On the other hand, in private owner organizations, it was found that a small fraction of
projects utilize most of the resources at the organization, which leads to expanding the pipeline of
projects into a longer time horizon. Furthermore, this leads the owner organization to prioritize
project execution according to their strategic impact. For example, it could be safety. A People
Manager at International Manufacturing Company said.
71
==PAGE:86==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”We are running multiple projects; we have 130 plus projects at a given time, so it is a long queue. If
you do the Pareto analysis, 8 to 10 projects occupy 80% of the resources or budget. So the rest is a long
queue of smaller projects. So when it comes to key account managers, investment requests, or any idea,
they have a priority list that they look at; if it has to make a safety improvement, it has top priority. ”
People Manager - International Manufacturing Company
Moreover, an impressive finding concerning project selection and strategic processing was
that data-driven models are being utilized in some owner organizations, such as Danish Railway
Client. Such models are being used to manage asset life by analyzing data from the asset, which
outputs predictions on when an asset needs maintenance.
”We have a model that measures the asset lifetime. We have some models for each element of the asset.
For example, how long can we expect sleepers, rails, ballasts, bridges and so forth to live, and we have a
model that tells us exactly for each element of our infrastructure, how old is it, and when we can expect a
big work to be necessary on it. This model can be running using different things of finance and stuff like
that, and then it tells us, OK, you might probably in 2027 have to do a project on the line from Aalborg
to Frederikshavn for example. ”
Section Manager B - Danish Railway Client Client

4.1.4
ESG
Environmental, Social, and corporate governance (ESG) are broad topics. Only the envi-
ronmental and social aspects were covered in this capability, as the corporate governance part
is mainly well discussed under the governance dimension. This strategic capability was induced
from the inductive coding analysis performed on the interviews held with the industry practi-
tioners. The capability entails three main CSFs, which are basically revolving around owner or-
ganizations’ need to integrate environmental awareness and social responsibility in their strategic
decisions and operations. The latter CSF combines both environmental and social consideration,
which is based on the ability of the owner to provide a safe working environment for construction
workers and their employees.
One of the major findings regarding environmental awareness is that many owner organiza-
tions nowadays have recognized the necessity of including environmental sustainability in their
corporate strategy.
”At the moment our strategy aims for low carbon emission, so we have reasonably substantial funding
and not trying to execute within this because of all the constraints that can be difficult to use enough
money. So that is why it is a kind of different approach if you were a private investor, but it is not easy
to do all the projects within the frames that we have to work in. ”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client Client
One of the aspects of environmental sustainability that could be included is implementing
green engineering solutions in the construction projects, whether it is in the design or construc-
tion phase of the project. This was emphasized by People Manager at International Manufactur-
ing Company.
”At a previous project in Norway we came up with this idea, which is part of our sustainability goals to
change old coke from burning coke to get the energy to melt the stone into an environmentally friendly
electrical version. ”
People Manager - International Manufacturing Company
Moreover, environmental sustainability could also be implemented within the owner orga-
73
==PAGE:88==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
nization’s supply chain. For example, a program manager at International Pharmaceutical com-
pany mentioned their strategic direction in implementing what they call ”Green Procurement
Strategy.”
”Green procurement is a very important capability, which actually means how to secure sustainable
sourcing. Especially at International Pharmaceutical Company is so important as a very large
organization. At International Pharmaceutical Company, we request from the suppliers to provide
documentation about the green and sustainable production of the supply. ”
Program Manager - International Pharmaceutical Company
”Green procurement is more important than cost, but less important than quality. ”
Program Manager - International Pharmaceutical Company
In addition to that, another important finding concerning the owner organization is the
important role in taking social responsibility in their construction projects. At Danish Railway
Client, project decisions are taken by prioritizing minimal social impact over project cost.
” I think in terms of price it would probably be better, but in terms of time it would be worse. As I said
in the triangle, time always wins with us because every time we need another day, there are no trains.
Moreover, if you take into account the Society cost of closing the railway is enormous. So we can afford
maybe to pay a little bit more in the direct costs of the projects to avoid these track closures. ”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client Client

4.1.5
Knowledge Management
Knowledge management is a strategic capability induced from the inductive coding analy-
sis performed on the interviews with industry practitioners. The capability entails the ability of
the owner organization to manage all the knowledge at the organizational level. This capabil-
ity was found to be driven by two main CSFs. The first is the organization’s ability to attract
human resources who possess needed competencies at the organization. The other is the owner
organization’s ability to manage and develop specific engineering or discipline knowledge to fa-
cilitate operations and growth in the organizational context. The latter was clearly mentioned as
challenge according to Section manager B at the Danish Railway Client,
”We need to have a lot higher level of discipline knowledge. So that is one of the things that we are
working on. Actually, my department was founded to solve this. ”
Section Manager B - Danish Railway Client
Regarding the first CSF, it was found that the industry, in general, lacks competent human
resources in project management who are able to have a cross-functional overview between the
different disciplines, such as engineering, legislative matters, organizational matters, and business
understanding of the vision and mission of the owner organization. This was highly emphasized
by Industrial PhD. at the Building Construction Client.
”Basically, if we are able to get the right human resources, that is, our project managers. For instance,
having the right level of competence, doing project management, and understanding the organizational
and legislative framework or context of how we build and why we build and basically what our
organization’s objectives are, we are quite far. That is one of the major challenges most organizations
would have right now, as there is a high demand on high competent employees, and that might be
difficult to get. ”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
