4.2
Commercial Dimension
The second dimension to be discussed is the commercial dimension. Commercial capabilities
refer to the organizational competencies needed for an owner organization to interface with its
suppliers. The owner organization’s commercial capabilities have an essential role in the collabo-
ration between the organization and its suppliers. Also, the commercial capabilities shed light on
how the organization works with project-based organizations such as suppliers and consultants.
It can be seen below, the findings from the primary data included the following capabilities as
commercial capabilities, which an owner organization must develop to become a SO organiza-
tion.
1. Packaging
2. Procurement
3. Authority and Legal Processing
4. Relational
5. Globalization
4.2.1
Packaging
According to the findings of the literature, packaging capability focus on the capabilities
needed for an owner organization to split large work packages into smaller ones or combine
smaller projects into larger ones to make them more attractive for suppliers to bid (G. Winch &
Leiringer, 2016). For an owner organization, packaging capabilities play an essential role, as they
76
==PAGE:91==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
support the organization in defining its commercial strategy towards the suppliers from the early
beginning of the collaboration.
Based on the data analyzed from the literature and industry interviews, the following critical
CSFs of an organization owner were identified. The CSFs are the contracting strategy and the
interfaces between packages. Contracting strategy defines the ability of the owner to split or
combine the project wok packages (WBS) into market-friendly clusters of work (G. Winch &
Leiringer, 2016). The interfaces between packages define what the owner needs to manage,
whilst interfaces within the packages are the supplier’s responsibility for that element of scope
and are formally "blind" to the owner.
One of the most major findings was that an owner organization needs to align with project
stakeholders about the project’s needs. This way, the owner organization can efficiently and
effectively identify the project’s needs. In that way, the organization can also maneuver when
work packages need to be broken down into smaller ones or combined into larger ones to lure
suppliers into bidding. As a Program Manager at the International Pharmaceutical Company
said.
”For that particular project to work, we do the RFP’s to link up with the stakeholders to figure out our
needs for this particular project. Then, afterward, we are doing the market analysis, finding out who can
supply to meet that need. Also, that can be, of course, super-advanced and hard to gauge, because if you
have a very advanced project, a massive one, it is difficult to know whether those capabilities are there for
all the market actors that are willing to bid on the project.”
Program Manager - International Pharmaceutical Company
One more finding was that the organization needs to create a contracting strategy for every
project in its portfolio. In order to do so, the people responsible for the project can collaborate,
including the legal department and the project leaders, to discuss the scope, purpose, strategy, the
desirable outcome, risks, success factors criteria, and relevant information about the execution
of the project. As the Senior manager at Danish Municipality and the Industrial Ph.D. at the
Building Construction Client mentioned.
77
==PAGE:92==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”We always have a contracting strategy for every project. So it starts with us making the contracting
strategy for the actual project. Then, we sit together with the lawyer or project leader to discuss the risks
in this project. What is the actual content of the project? What are the success criteria? How do we want
to promote it to have the best process for the project and the best product in the end?”
Senior manager - Danish Municipality
”It is helpful to have an overall strategy for each project. The organization would need to have a specific
strategy for that project.”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
Based on section Manager A of the Danish railway client, the time constraints at the Danish
railway field is vital for the project execution. Therefore, the organization tends to combine small
packages into larger packages to attract large suppliers who can supply almost everything needed.
In that way, the organization can easier coordinate the activities between the organization and
the suppliers, especially due to the low number of suppliers. As a result, valuable time can be saved.
However, a challenge found that the organization could even tender in smaller packages, but the
organization would need more time to do so.
One more finding from the section manager A depicts the importance of the organization
to combine or optimally split tenders to execute projects on time with the lowest risk, best price,
best quality.
”We have several different models that we try to apply. However, we tend to combine tenders more
because, as I mentioned, we usually have very tight time constraints in the execution phase of our
projects. So we cannot coordinate different suppliers into our projects. So we try to combine the tenders to
have large suppliers that supply everything. Then, of course, they can use subcontractors, but then they
need to have the capability to move around with all these different suppliers. Maybe we could tender in
smaller or specific packages, but we would need more time.”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
Another finding showed that there are two ways to successful packaging. First, using models
such as stage-gate models supports the execution progress of a project. Second, by well-defining
78
==PAGE:93==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
the interfaces between packages. This leads to better collaboration with the suppliers since it is
very clear for the suppliers what the requirements for the supply and the process are, in other
words, what-how-when they need to deliver.
”We have two ways of packaging. First, in project phases, we execute this project gate model. So we have
the idea phase, concept, design, and engineering phase implementation phase and handover phase, 5
phases. Regarding the scope of supply, we also have very clear interfaces between part project managers
who deliver what and which equipment, which pieces of the equipment belong to which group. Also,
these people have very clearly defined internal packages for their part of delivery. Suppliers appreciate
and give flexibility because sometimes, not all suppliers can deliver complete turnkey delivery.”
Program Manager - International Pharmaceutical Company
Another challenge found that there is a shortage of competent people and machinery available
in Denmark all year round except in summer.
”For contractors, most work is done in the summer. This is a problem because how can we have the same
contractor who has only a limited number of people and skills and machines available. So if we do more
than one project at a time, then contractors find it difficult to mobilize for it.”
Section Manager B - Danish Railway Client

4.2.2
Procurement
The literature review defines procurement capabilities as identifying, selecting, and moti-
vating suppliers that draw on deep commercial capabilities of understanding the supply market
structure. This is undoubtedly a vital owner competence. This commercial capability entails
the following six main CSFs. First, the bidding defines the success factors on how owner or-
ganizations manage the bidding process with the suppliers. Second, negotiation and agreements
define how the owner organization executes negotiation. Third, purchasing procedure including
tasks and plans in relevant procurement documents and staff. Forth, supplier selection defines the
qualification and regulation of supplier selection. Fifth, procurement contract management de-
fines the management of the obligation and the right of both parties during the execution of the
contract establishes principles of benefit sharing and risk-sharing. Sixth, procurement insourc-
ing or outsourcing determines the assignment of projects internally or externally by the owner
organization.
The first finding noted in this section regarding the commercial capability in procurement
dictates that cost is an important criterion for bidding. More importantly, the competencies of
the project-based firm to solve the project and their strategy behind this.
”We invited the market to a market dialogue, saying that now we will make another new contract,
meaning that we are setting up new criteria for the bidding. For instance, the price is a counting 25%, but
the most crucial matter for us is to ask them if they can explain how they will solve the project and why
they are thinking about these?”
Senior manager B - Danish Regional Client
Moreover, green procurement is an extremely important criterion for the owner organi-
zation when it comes to supplier selection. In order to secure green sourcing, the organization
demands from its suppliers to state under a document their green and sustainable production of
the supply.
80
==PAGE:95==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”Green procurement is a very important capability, which actually means how to secure sustainable
sourcing. Especially at International Pharmaceutical Company is so important as a very large
organization. At International Pharmaceutical Company, we request from the suppliers to provide
documentation about the green and sustainable production of the supply.”
Program manager - International Pharmaceutical Company
It is worth noting that due to the EU regulations, there are more regulations in the public
sector than the private sector. A challenge found in the public sector is that complying with all
these additional regulations requires a tremendous amount of time; thus, there is not sufficient
time for procurement activities to find the best supplier. In the private sector, supplier selection
mostly occurs based on previous experience or collaboration with the supplier.
”There are regulations such as EU regulations saying how the organization should make its procurement
in the public sector, and it is very rule-based and limited. Sometimes this legal structure takes too much
of the organization’s attention, so that is the commercial aspect of getting the best suppliers to throw the
task and so on; it takes too little time. In the public sector, there is a tendency for the organization to
spend most of its time complying to some regulations and thus, the commercial aspects are too limited. In
the private sector, it is based on past experience with the suppliers that guides the organization in
supplier selection.”
Director - Danish Owners Association
Another major challenge found indicates that the organization typically uses a standard pro-
curement process for all projects without considering the different needs of the project from the
market.
81
==PAGE:96==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”We sometimes see that one organization has sort of one standard procurement formula. So rather than
understanding the market and this specific task before going to market, they sort of more automatically
choose the same form of procurement as they did the last time. So I guess it is fair to say that there is a
need for improvement to be more open to what the market has to offer and be more strategic in the
organization structure and its work. Moreover, there is a considerable variation from the worst to the best
clients here.”
Director - Danish Owners Association
One more major finding, nowadays the organizations mainly work together with one sup-
plier who can supply almost everything. In that way, the risk is allocated to this supplier, so the
money as well.
”Nowadays, the organization’s contract with a total contractor in the Danish construction market means
fewer suppliers. It was more of a trade contact in the past, and I expect it will be like that ten years from
now. Risk is moved to the Total contractor, so the money as the responsibility is moved to their side.”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
Another finding, based on the section manager A of the Danish railway client was that it is
critical for the organization to be able to define the scope of its project and manage its advisors.
”We will need to be able to allow ourselves to define the scope of these projects and to manage our
advisors in a way the projects that will end up tendering out are contractible and can meet the the quality
and financial goals of the project.”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
A finding was that the organization could acquire a system of tenders where the selection
of suppliers is based on well-defined criteria such as experience, financial strength, and organiza-
tional skills. When the suppliers fulfill these requirements, they could be allocated to the so-called
qualified supplier list. In addition, tenders that are included in the list most of the time are to be
processed.
82
==PAGE:97==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”We have a tendering system where we have a list of qualified companies. A supplier should be on the
eligible list to participate in the tendering. This is also related to bidding. We can also use public tenders
once in a while where the companies not on the list can tender. However, they would have to meet
criteria to document their experience, financial strength, organizational capabilities. However, in most
cases, we tender to the qualified list.”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client

4.2.3
Authority and Legal Processing
Authority and legal processing define the owner’s involvement in facilitating authority ap-
provals for projects. This commercial capability entails the following two main CSFs. The first
one defines the owners’ knowledge, use, and involvement in the tender regulations, contracts,
or legal agreements to comply with the rules and norms of the national and international stan-
dards. The second one defines the owner’s role in facilitating the approval of decisions, legal, and
authority matters of the project.
83
==PAGE:98==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
The first finding noted in this section was that the owner organization have no choice but
to comply with the national and international standards set for construction projects delivery.
”I think this is very regulated by law. So we do not have too many other options. Of course, there is the
possibility of tendering by negotiation, but it is also a very strictly governed process. Moreover, I think we
do not have too much experience with that in Danish Railway Client. We tend to do it more
traditionally with a tender that its price combined with other sub-criteria. ”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
Living up to the set legislation regarding contract tendering is an essential criterion for the
owner organization. According to Industrial Ph.D. at Building Construction Client, more com-
plexity has been added to the European legislation, making it more challenging for the owner
organization to manage their contracts.
”Basically, the first and foremost criteria of doing this successfully would then be to live up to this
legislation, because if you enter into a contract with a private supplier and you have not lived up to their
requirements in that contract, it can be terminated. So you will get fined and you will also be subject to
some kind of legal prosecution. That is a harsh term that it is expensive. So first and foremost, live up to
legislation which is quite extensive in this aspect in recent years there has been added more and more
complexity to some of the tendering legislation in Europe ”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
In addition to that, another finding was regarding the approvals in many owner organizations
that follow a stage gate model concept, as is the case at Danish Railway Client according to section
manager A. However it does not mean that the owner organization is in anyway bounded to these
gates, as project decisions are made without senior approvals in some situations to avoid schedule
delays in the project.
84
==PAGE:99==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”We operate in an adapted Prince 2 model with a stage-gate model. Very traditional waterfall project
model. Maybe it seems a logical choice in the infrastructure projects. But it is an adapted Prince 2
version because we cannot pause a project for a gate, so we tend to operate alongside the decision process.
Then we have the milestones plan that we monitor on a more fluent basis. On a weekly basis, we have a
board meeting. But we go into the milestones that are either delayed or at risk of being delayed. ”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
As a last finding regarding approvals, it was found that there are usually several phases pre-
ceding the project start date in the Danish market of the construction industry. Therefore, the
more involvement there is from the owner’s side, the faster the processes will be. The section
manager at Danish Municipality mentioned this.
”In this contract, we have an organization that orders projects with us. That contract says that they have
to say yes or no to going further after the planning phase. Then the next package we should deliver is
what we call ”Dispositionsforslag” And they also have to say OK. However, after that, it is, in theory,
just us going ahead with the project after that approval.”
Senior Manager - Danish Municipality

4.2.4
Relational
Based on the reviewed literature, relational capabilities refer to the capabilities needed for
the owner to collaborate with commercial parties effectively. Relational capabilities are identified
85
==PAGE:100==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
as vital owner capabilities. Traditionally, the desired relationships between the parties are stated
under a formal and legal agreement. Also, (Poppo & Zenger, 2002; Gil et al., 2011) argue that
interpersonal relationships are the basis for practical cooperation between the parties, so the con-
tract can be based on the top to govern business behaviors. Based on (G. M. Winch, 2009), it
is worth noting as a reminder that the owner organizations have to comply with the local and
international legislation as well. Therefore, the relational capabilities may differ from country to
country and from region to region.
Based on the literature analysis and industry interviews, four main CSFs for this important
owner capability were identified: working relationship, innovation, supplier management, and
culture. First, working relationship refers to the cooperation relationship of the owner organiza-
tion with the suppliers. Second, innovation indicates the new ideas and methods for effective and
efficient cooperation between the organization’s owner and the suppliers. Third, supplier man-
agement determines the procedure to ensure that the owner organization receives the maximum
value for money that an organization pays to its suppliers. Last but not least, culture where the
owner organization values the reliability, truthfulness, and ability of suppliers to work together
transparently.
The first important finding was that the organization needs to value the different cultural
aspects of its organization and its suppliers. Furthermore, it is highlighted that trust is critical
for collaboration between the organization and its suppliers to succeed in projects. Also, trust
between project participants is underestimated in Denmark and is not considered when setting
contracts. Last but not least, it is super important for all the involved parties of the projects that
they have success after the completion of the project in terms of monetary accomplishments.
Based on the Industrial PhD of the Building Construction Client, it is challenging to make
a great success of a project if at least one party has lost money or reputation during the project’s
lifetime. However, one challenge found that if the organization does not have trust between the
main contractual parts, and if the people responsible for the contract on each side of the table start
having a conflict there, it would be complicated to get some project success.
86
==PAGE:101==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”Most of the time, trust is probably undervalued in most projects. In how we define contracts in
Denmark, they are not as specific as they would be in other countries and the best projects. Also, I cannot
point to any specific research on this, but I would say that most successful projects would be successful for
all parties involved. It is challenging to make a great success if at least one party has lost money on being
part of it, or lost reputation or something else. Moreover, if the organization does not have trust between
the main contractual parts, and if the people responsible for the contract on each side of the table start
having a conflict there, it would be complicated to get some project success.”
Industrial PhD - Building Construction Client
Section manager A mentioned as a challenge that the existing models that measure the quality
the performance of the contractors depend on the contractors’ feedback. Therefore, it is extremely
difficult to measure the quality of contractors’ deliverables, as there are no defined models that
can do this independently for contractors.
”It is difficult to find a model to measure the quality of contractors because they can write what they
really want and yet not deliver. But, at the same time, the money involved is so large that we must also
reduce the price as much as possible to make the best use of taxpayers’ money. However, it is a problem
that sometimes contractors get the wrong motivation. ”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
According to the Director of the Danish owners association, nowadays, there are some moves
towards a more focused collaboration between organizations, but the level of collaboration is not
sufficient yet. Also, one more finding showed that the need for innovation is very important for
the organizations.
87
==PAGE:102==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”There are some moves towards a more focused collaboration between organizations, and so on, but there
are still many professional people around who have somehow not seen the light on new ways of working.
And that’s really harmful if you’re doing a project and you’re saying that’s how we want things to be and
what you’re getting is something completely different, that’s very difficult.”
Director - Danish Owners Association
”So, even if our organization is full of people who are really ready to deliver simple projects, we must
also always think about how we can function more intelligently.”
Director - Danish Owners Association
Finally, the director of Section B highlighted that strategic partnerships could reduce risk
and offer the best price.
”I think it terms of getting the lowest risk at the best price I think strategic partnerships could be a good
option.”
Section Manager B - Danish Railway Client

4.2.5
Globalization
Globalization is a commercial capability induced from the inductive coding analysis per-
formed on the interviews with industry practitioners. Globalization entails that the owner or-
ganization must collaborate with international suppliers besides the national suppliers. The or-
ganization needs to be flexible to work with international suppliers worldwide. In this way, the
organization can ensure better prices, better quality, new knowledge, and new skills. The find-
ings presented are based on the analyzed data from the industry interviews. One main CSF for
this important owner capability was identified. International collaboration CSF defines the co-
operation between the owner organization and the international suppliers.
Based on the senior manager B of Danish Regional Client and section manager A of Dan-
ish Railway Client, the international suppliers or contractors can bring additional value to the
Danish market, especially in short-term projects. Furthermore, they confirmed a need to start
collaborating more with international suppliers since they can fill Denmark’s existing shortage
of machinery, skills, and competencies.
”When international contractors come into the Danish market with another angle in other competence, I
hope they can change the Danish market. This is because I am stunned over how the main part of the
contractor does not understand that there is a need to change. The international contractors are coming
into Denmark and working in total order anyway.”
Senior manager B - Danish Regional Client
”I think there is an issue with the business itself. It is a kind of a niche market, so there are not too many
suppliers. There are also some shortages in specific machinery and specific competencies. Sometimes we
can experience extremely poor competition on some of our projects, and it is a very difficult thing to work
within the short term. Actually, it is impossible. Maybe it is something that we can affect in the long
term, but it is not easy. So we are in a market that’s a bit too narrow.”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client

4.3
Governance Dimension
The third dimension to be discussed is the governance dimension. Based on the literature
review, governance capabilities are mainly those organizational competencies required for an
organization to become a strong owner organization to interface successfully with a temporary
project organization (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016).
The findings from the primary data included the following capabilities as governance capa-
bilities, which an owner organization must develop to become a SO organization.
1. Assurance
2. Project coordination
3. Asset management
4.3.1
Assurance
As depicted in the literature review, assurance capabilities focus on the internal activities
an organization manages to inform the executive management about the progress of a project
(National Audit Office, 2010). As the project progresses, the project leaders need to know who
is responsible for making decisions, what these decisions are about, and when these decisions are
made. To do this, these four CSFs are important for the organization to ensure effective project
assurance. Firstly, communication helps to exchange information. Communication includes in-
formation exchange, and cost data transparency. Secondly, compliance defines the ability to act
on a mandate, a set of rules or requests that set practices and goals. Thirdly, ownership definition
determines the person’s assignment responsible for an action or activity. Fourthly, owner orga-
90
==PAGE:105==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
nizational structure defines the owner’s ability to create an organizational structure connecting
all project parties together.
One of the first major findings regarding assurance is that the project’s progress is reported
to the board at meetings on the project milestones. However, a major challenge identified from
section manager A, where the project continues and moves to the next phase even though the
prerequired phase is not completed yet. That indicates a great risk, but as section manager A
argued, stopping the project is even more significant.
”We operate in an adapted Prince 2 model with a stage-gate model. Very traditional waterfall project
model. However, it is an adapted Prince 2 version because we cannot pause a project for a gate, so we
operate alongside the decision process. Then we have the milestones plan that we monitor on a more
fluent basis. Every week we have a board meeting where the agenda is about the milestones that are
either delayed or in risk of being delayed.”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
”The difference is that we do not stop and wait for approval. We always keep on. So corrective actions
from the gate will then be implemented later, and that of at this has a risk to it, but it is the risk, by
stopping is bigger.”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
Another finding from section manager A and the program manager indicates that it is essen-
tial for the organization to effectively document the ownership definition, the roles of the project
participants, and the organization’s structure.
”It is very effectively documented who does what in the project organization, and of course, we also have
stakeholder roles for them.”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
In addition, another finding by the Senior Manager B of Danish Regional client shows the
importance of the involvement of the various parts of the organization during and after the
project. Especially, when it comes to the definition of the roles and the responsibilities of the
employees.
91
==PAGE:106==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”It is important for the organization to have clear ownership roles for the individuals. Also, it is essential
to have clear, defined responsibilities and area of responsibility as well.”
Senior manager B - Danish Regional Client

4.3.2
Project Coordination
Project coordination governance capability addresses the need of the owner organization to
secure the ongoing progress of the project’s life cycle. In that way, the owner organizations could
enhance their competencies of what is needed to execute projects more effectively by having a
thorough understanding of other project parties’ needs. To begin with, in order to secure effective
project coordination across the involved parties of the project, the work processes needs to be
adopted by the parties efficiently. Project coordination was found to be driven by the following
three main CSFs. The first is cooperation efficiency which describes the project participants’
willingness to cooperate and the effect on information exchange. The second one is responsibility
definition which describes the owner organization’s ability to define responsibilities between all
project participants. The last CSF is monitoring and controlling, which refers to the owner’s
effective control and measurement of project performance over its lifetime.
92
==PAGE:107==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
One of the first findings regarding project coordination, is that it seems that there is a certain
competency that is missing in the industry in general. This competency is what section manager B
from the Danish Railway client named as ”engineering management”. Engineering management
is a discipline that is set to cross-coordinate between different functions in an organization, such
as, engineering, finance, etc.
”If we look at the internal aspect of the project, contract management is something we are looking into a
lot, especially with ABR18 regime. One of the things that we are developing more, so we can kind of
use a lot, is engineering management (teknisk projektledelse) and looking at the cross-discipline lead of
the project, so you know where the project management is very focused on conflict management, finances,
stuff like that, you need somebody coordinating the different disciplines and the deliverables between the
different disciplines, usually called engineering management. I think that is also quite vital. ”
Section Manager B - Danish Railway Client
Moreover, communication and clear role definitions between the stakeholders was found
to be critical in having successful project coordination. The owner needs to be able to involve
stakeholders at the right time needed in the project, to have an optimal, and smooth project
process. The senior manager at Danish Municipality well emphasized this.
”I talked about these partnership contracts with the organization that orders projects. We have tried to
describe roles and responsibilities, and if that is clear for all the stakeholders involved, and we are able to
draw a line of when do we have the meeting, what are the content of the meetings, then we have a good
flow. We are working with that all the time, and a lot of projects we have does have an OK, smooth
process, and everybody thinks that they were drawn into the project at the right times and have made
their contribution to making the right decisions, and sometimes it just goes forth and back, because the
right people have not been part of the project on the right time”
Senior Manager - Danish Municipality
The importance of communication was also highlighted by senior manager B at Danish
Regional Client. The owner organization needs to have the ability to create a unique project
culture where trust, honesty, and transparency are the core of the project environment.
93
==PAGE:108==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
”When I look into the different projects, we have this problem with communication and collaboration
between the contractors and the construction management. The collaboration between the client
organization and the consultants and so forth. We are looking into lots of different projects and my
conclusion is that we have to have the right culture and mindsets. ”
Senior Manager B - Danish Regional Client
”I think transparency it is super important so that everyone knows more or less what is going on. Trust
could be another main thing as well.”
Program Manager - International Pharmaceutical Company
A final finding concerning project coordination was that data-driven project monitoring and
controlling would improve transparency between all project stakeholders. According to Director
at Danish Owners Association, digitization of project logging would be an excellent next step in
the industry.
” I guess one of the big problems is that we talk a lot about digital information and data and so on. But
so far most of digitisation and data has been in the forum of design. Whereas when it comes to logging,
what is actually going on in a project between the parties, we lack data. A really nice, next step would be
to get the facts right.”
Director - Danish Owners Association

4.3.3
Asset Management
As defined in the literature review, asset integration addresses the capabilities required for an
organization to integrate the final asset into its existing operation. In addition, it was essential to
consider the flexibility of the asset to change over time. In view of these two CSFs, the ability to
manage assets is vital to the organization, as the activities followed after the creation of the final
asset are considered.
The first important finding was that the organization should consider the differences between
the design and the actual execution phases - buildability. Also, another finding indicates that the
use of modern technologies such as 3D coordination and building information models help tackle
many of the problems.
”The possibility of building controls how a building is built in the field on a rainy day, which is always
difficult to coordinate in the design phase. Usually, designers have not seen many projects done in real
life. We spend much time on 3D coordination and the BIM coordination and this is a key focus. So a lot
of our strategic work is to use more BIM to solve many of these problems we have.”
Section Manager B - Danish Railway Client
Another significant finding determines that it is essential for the organization to obtain ac-
curate data regarding asset integration.
”When the data is old and has not been updated. Also, when they are not accurate. Accuracy is a real
issue.”
Section Manager A - Danish Railway Client
Another important finding, especially for property owners, is the fact that procedures should
be put in place regarding the delivery of the project on how to integrate the asset into the existing
business operation.
95
==PAGE:110==

”When we complete one construction project, there is a whole standard procedure of just handing over the
responsibility of that finished building to our maintenance department because we are also the property
owner.”

5
Development of the Maturity Model Framework
In this section of the report, the developed OCMM was discussed. Based on the findings
presented and interpreted in the previous section, the outcome was used to establish the OCMM.
OCMM is a maturity assessment excel developed tool intended to be used by owner organizations
to assess their maturity level on each capability dimension (strategic, commercial, governance). A
guide on how to use the tool is provided in it. The methodology for building up the framework
is the following.
• Using the reviewed literature and interviews, a capability map was developed, including
capabilities and CSFs for the three defined dimensions.
• The maturity model structure was developed using the reviewed models in the literature.
• After developing the needed structure, the maturity levels were defined, relating to the
research topic.
• Using the developed capability map and findings from the interviews, each capability was
defined in each maturity level. Each level of maturity included a set of maturity elements
that an organization needs to have to mature past this level. These maturity elements in-
cluded success factors, challenges found, use of enabling dimensions, and in some cases the
logical structure of the maturity model.
This process was further discussed in this section, where all the maturity levels for each
capability in the three dimensions was reflected upon so that further research can be done for the
continuous development of the model.
5.1
Maturity Model Levels Definition
This section will introduce the five different levels of the maturity model. Each level de-
scribes the progression of the capabilities inside an organization. The definitions were deduced
from other capability models presented in table 7 of the methodology section while the structure
follows a standard capability maturity model structure as it can be seen in figure 11, and presented
97
==PAGE:112==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
in the literature review section.
Figure 11: Standard Capability Maturity Model Levels
• Level 1: Initial
Initial level of maturity where the organization shows in general poor maturity in the de-
fined critical success factor of each capability, co-related to the three different capability
dimensions. The overall score in the OCMM assessment tool (0 - 20%)
• Level 2: Repeatable At this level, the organization will show a fair and managed performance
in the defined critical success factor of each capability, co-related to the three different
capability dimensions. However, it still shows major weaknesses in many CSF. The overall
score in the OCMM assessment tool (20 - 40%)
• Level 3: Complete Organized At this level, the organization will show a good performance in
each capability’s defined critical success factor, co-related to the three different capability
dimensions. The recognition of the CSF in the owner organization would have significantly
raised, with some recognized weaknesses that the organization would be working on. The
overall score in the OCMM assessment tool (40 - 60%)
98
==PAGE:113==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
• Level 4: Quantitatively managed At this level of maturity, the capabilities defined are generally
very well applied and existent according to the CSF under each of them. Minor weaknesses
would still be present. However, collaborative cooperation, technical performance, cross-
organizational performance adjustment, and continuous development optimization are very
well established in the organization. The OCMM value is gradually reflected in the final
performance of the projects in the organization. The overall score in the OCMM assessment
tool (60 - 80%)
• Level 5: Optimizing At this final level of maturity, the owner organization would be consid-
ered strong in recognizing all the capabilities defined in CM, and would have an excellent
performance in most of the defined CSF. The organization’s management would apply pro-
grams to continue to optimize all the mechanisms in both the projects and the organization
in general. The overall score in the OCMM assessment tool (80 - 100%)
5.2
Strategic Dimension Maturity Levels Definition
5.2.1
Capital Raising
As discussed in the finding sections, the main drivers for thriving capital raising at any owner
organization are driven by strategic planning and political negotiations. At an initial level of
maturity in capital raising, an owner organization usually has no control over the type of projects
or the yearly capital volume, where the politicians mainly decide the project pipeline. Moreover,
findings showed that there seems to be no strategic planning or management in a formal sense,
where owner organizations tend to plan only on the tactical and operational levels. Furthermore,
environmental sustainability is not considered an asset at this initial level to attract more capital
for the projects.
At a more repeatable level, the capital volume of projects fluctuates from one year to another
due to only short-term planning. This is mainly due to the lack of a clear strategic direction of
the owner organization, defining the type of projects it undertakes. At this level of maturity,
it can be argued that some parts of successful planning and strategic performance management
are being used. However, it is uneven and sometimes ineffective. Planning discipline is unlikely
99
==PAGE:114==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
to be strict because it occurs solely in response to events. Moreover, the sustainability enabling
dimensions starts becoming an initiative for raising more funds at the owner organization. In
addition, the owner organization starts developing competencies for impacting political decisions
by applying the fundamentals of active leadership discussed in the literature review.
On a more defined and organized level of maturity, the owner organization starts consid-
ering planning for a longer horizon in a defined diversity of project types and a yearly capital
volume. Moreover, strategic planning becomes more structured but remains uncertain due to
projects’ vulnerability in delays. Formal procedures and processes start taking place, and projects’
environmental sustainability is considered an incentive for more project funds and capital volume.
In addition to that, the political influence increases, and the owner organization starts collaborat-
ing with the government when deciding the project pipeline.
The CSFs become better managed and especially quantitatively managed at a more mature
level using a systems engineering approach. At this stage, the owner organization has a con-
trolled yearly capital volume for diverse projects, depending on its portfolio of services. More-
over, decision-making is driven by strategy. Organizational-wide norms and methodologies are
widely used for strategy management, and the owner’s strategic vision defines the project pipeline
with a high collaboration efficiency with governmental officials. Regarding environmental sus-
tainability, the owner organization has matured to use sustainable solutions as a selling point for
better quality projects leading to more capital volume for executing tasks. Therefore, the appli-
cation of sustainable leadership and systems engineering is decisive at this stage.
Finally, in an optimized level of maturity, the owner organization focuses on optimizing the
diversity in the project portfolio without risking the sensitivity of the capital volume fluctuations
from one year to another. Moreover, the strategic project planning at the owner organization is
not severely affected by projects delays or by re-prioritization of projects. Moreover, the yearly
budget is well defined and flexible in transferring capital between calendar years. Furthermore,
actively being an industry leader, the owner organization focuses on environmental and social
sustainability in getting more funds supporting all projects in the portfolio. Similarly, the political
direction of the municipality or the government does not much affect the project pipeline at the
100
==PAGE:115==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
owner organization.
The above-discussed maturity levels of the strategic capability in capital raising is summa-
rized in figure 12.
Figure 12: Capital Raising Maturity Levels Definition
5.2.2
Stakeholder Management
According to the findings of this study, the main drivers for stakeholder management is a
defined approach for communication and base the project decisions in a more user-oriented way.
At an initial stage of maturity, the owner has difficulty determining all the necessary stakeholders
to be involved in the project. This is mainly due to the fact that, when project stakeholders change
their representatives in a project frequently, the owner is not informed. This is caused by poor
communication efficiency between all project parties.
At a more repeatable level, stakeholders management starts getting more manageable where
defining the right project stakeholders by using stakeholder mapping, or landscaping is developed
at an early stage of the project, so the client knows when to involve each stakeholder. However,
project decisions from the owner’s side is severely influenced by the balance between the benefits
101
==PAGE:116==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
and the threats to the stakeholders.
Maturity level 3 characterizes a more defined and organized stakeholder management ap-
proach at the owner organization. One of the main things that start getting in order internally
in the temporary project organization is communication efficiency, as project stakeholders take
responsibility for their part of the project, and the interface between the duties is well managed.
One of the main enablers actively involved in this maturity state is leadership, as the owner is
actively involved in following up on all stakeholders and driving the project forward. Moreover,
information management and communication starts getting a defined structure at the organiza-
tion.
As the owner organization matures, the CSFs become more managed in a quantifiable man-
ner, as leadership is still empowered at the owner’s side, as well as project organizing starts taking
more effective structures subjective to each project. One of the main characteristics of this phase
is the application of project 13 organizational structure explained in the literature review section
leading to building a close and managed relationship between the owner and the different stake-
holders. Furthermore, at this stage, the owner has worked on an approach for communicating
reliable information to the various project stakeholders, creating a more transparent and honest
collaboration.
Finally, as the organizational maturity level in stakeholder management reaches level 5, the
challenges found in this capability at the different maturity levels get resolved. First, the owner
organization makes sure of early involvement of the various stakeholders in the project, consider-
ing their point of view. Moreover, decisions become more user-oriented and do not affect project
benefits. In addition, effective use of leadership and project organizing enabling dimensions leads
to politicians supporting and backing up the owner organization’s decisions.
102
==PAGE:117==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
The above-discussed maturity levels of the strategic capability in stakeholder management
is summarized in figure 13.
Figure 13: Stakeholder Management Maturity Levels Definition
5.2.3
Portfolio Management
According to the findings, portfolio management is primarily driven by the owner’s ability
to select the most beneficial projects to be included in the portfolio and define a clear project
mission indicating the project’s goals, purpose, and objectives. Moreover, the owner needs to
have a strategic role in project decisions from the early phases of the project.
At the initial maturity phase, the owner organization struggles in defining the scope and the
right projects’ requirements and has a poor overview in selecting the suitable projects to enter the
portfolio. This is mainly due to the complexity behind defining the correct requirements from
the idea phase to the execution phase.
At a more repeatable maturity level, the complexity of projects is realized, and the owner
organization sets resources into defining the correct requirements of the projects. Moreover, the
selection process becomes more standardized, especially in public organizations, as the selection
of projects is made according to the political will.
103
==PAGE:118==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
In a more organized and defined maturity level, the owner applies active leadership in in-
volving all project stakeholders in defining the project requirements and definition in the early
stages. Moreover, project goal definition has to be aligned with the organization’s strategy, and
standard procedures are defined for defining the project’s right requirements. In addition to that,
at this level of maturity, the organization starts considering long-term planning of the project
pipeline as a strategic alternative next to short-term planning.
As the maturity levels advance to level 4, systems engineering is utilized in selecting the
projects entering the portfolio for a calendar year. The complexity in the projects has declined
as every project scope is being well defined in collaboration with all the project’s stakeholders.
Furthermore, at this level, the owner organization must have a developed or mature level of
strategic planning so that the yearly capital volume is well managed. The use of a standardized
project management approach results in homogeneous data, which is beneficial for analysis.
At the most optimal maturity level, the owner organization has a controlled level of com-
plexity of the projects, which leads to defining the correct requirement of the projects. Moreover,
project selection is a matter of coordination between the political will and the owner’s will. The
owner fits the project according to its financial possibilities and each project’s value creation. In
addition, the innovative technological advancement internally in the organization is at a mature
state, so data-driven tools for project selection are used for several organizational strategic pur-
poses.
104
==PAGE:119==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
The above discussed maturity levels of the strategic capability in portfolio management is
summarized in figure 14.
Figure 14: Portfolio Management Maturity Levels Definition
5.2.4
ESG
ESG is a hot topic in the corporate environment nowadays, as companies are striving to
implement environmental sustainability, social responsibility, and corporate governance in their
business strategies. At an initial phase of maturity, it is argued that owner organizations have
no environmental awareness or considerations regarding that matter in projects decisions. This
is mainly due to the poor maturity level at the organization, as management priorities at that
stage are instead on defining profitable business models and developing their commercial and
governance capabilities for building a more mature business environment.
At a more repeatable maturity level, the organization would have matured in many of the
other essential business capabilities that drive the organization’s growth. At this point, the en-
vironmental awareness in projects becomes a ”nice to have”, that could, for instance, the owner
organization realizes the potential in sustainable procurement. In a more defined and organized
maturity phase (level 3), environmental awareness in projects becomes a ”must-have”. The or-
105
==PAGE:120==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
ganization begins requiring sustainable engineering solutions from their suppliers and internally.
This is done simultaneously while applying the concept of active leadership explained in the lit-
erature review section.
As the organization matures to a more managed and controlled level. one of the main aspects
that becomes a critical KPI in the owner organization’s strategic direction is aiming for low carbon
emission projects. Moreover, The owner organization considers the social cost of its project
and starts taking social responsibility. On a higher and more optimal maturity level, the owner
organization’s strategy aims for carbon neutrality. Social responsibility is optimized, and every
project decision will have on society is systematically analyzed.
The above-discussed maturity levels of the strategic capability in ESG is summarized in figure
15.
Figure 15: ESG Maturity Levels Definition
5.2.5
Knowledge Management
Knowledge management was one of the capabilities induced from the analysis of the inter-
views. The capability entails the ability of the owner organization to manage all the knowledge at
the organizational level and the ability to manage and develop specific engineering or discipline
knowledge to facilitate operations and growth in the corporate context. At an initial maturity
106
==PAGE:121==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
stage, the owner organization has minimal resources and limited expertise and knowledge for
project delivery. As the organization matures to level two, resource constraint remains critical;
however, the organization still manages project delivery.
In a more organized and defined context of maturity, talent management through employee
training becomes a must at the organization, and the owner sets standards for the employee’s
knowledge level. This is done through active leadership from the management of the owner or-
ganization. Furthermore, knowledge management becomes more controllable when the orga-
nization reaches a more managed and controlled maturity state at level four. The owner organi-
zation successfully manages to remove the resource constraint in projects. For example, it could
be argued that the missing competency of engineering management discussed in the findings
section becomes mature at this stage of maturity. Finally, the organization develops knowledge
management for each discipline at an optimal maturity level. Talent management consistently
works with employees to develop needed knowledge and competencies for optimally executing
multiple projects.
107
==PAGE:122==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
The above-discussed maturity levels of the strategic capability in knowledge management is
summarized in figure 16.
Figure 16: Knowledge Management Maturity Levels Definition
5.3
Commercial Dimension Maturity Levels Definition
5.3.1
Packaging
As discussed in the findings section, the main drivers for packaging were the existence of a
defined approach to the contracting strategy and the interfaces between packages. The packag-
ing capabilities are highly linked with the work breakdown structure, which completes complex
projects with many moving parts step-by-step. Packaging capabilities play an important role
for an owner organization, as they support the organization early in defining their contracting
strategy.
At an initial level of maturity in packaging, an owner organization usually has no contract
strategy in a formal sense. The organization tends to plan only on a regular or business level in
an ad-hoc and uncontrolled manner, generally by senior management. Furthermore, findings
108
==PAGE:123==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
showed that there seem to be no project packages interconnection at that level but are highly
interdependent. If there is a delay on one package, the impact will directly affect other packages.
At a more repeatable level, the organization begins to develop a contracting strategy to con-
nect business needs with the needs of stakeholders. The organization conducts an informal pro-
curement strategy to generate market analysis to identify suppliers who can supply. Moreover,
the need for package interfaces is recognized, and a plan has been implemented to reduce delivery
delays.
On a more defined and organized level of maturity, the organization has a well-defined
contracting strategy to connect business needs with stakeholders’ needs. Furthermore, the orga-
nization conducts a formal contracting strategy to create market analysis to identify the suppliers
who can supply. In addition, the organization has a well-defined set of methods to combine or
split tenders to execute projects on time with the lowest risk, best price, best quality, sustainability.
Also, there are formal procedures for how packages are interconnected, and the organization sets
requirements on how delivery should not be affected by package delays. Finally, project organiz-
ing, systems engineering, and sustainability enabling dimensions start becoming an initiative for
having better interfaces between packages and contracting strategy at the owner organization.
At a more mature level, the organization possesses a manageable contracting strategy to con-
nect business needs with stakeholders’ needs. The organization conducts a contracting strategy
manageable to create market analysis to identify the suppliers who can supply. Moreover, the
organization manageable combines or splits tenders to execute projects on time with the lowest
risk, best price, best quality, sustainability. Also, interface processes are managed, the benefits of a
systematic interface begin to appear in the organization. However, it is still sometimes inefficient
the way that the organization operates. Therefore, there is still little room for improvement in
these areas.
Finally, in an optimized level of maturity, the organization possesses an optimal contracting
strategy to connect business needs with stakeholders’ needs. The organization conducts a con-
tracting strategy optimally to create market analysis to identify the suppliers who can supply. Also,
the organization optimally combines or splits tenders to optimally execute projects on time with
109
==PAGE:124==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
the lowest risk, best price, best quality, sustainability. Moreover, the optimized project interface
between packages and project participants always finds critical paths to delivery. Therefore, the
organization operates optimally by being effective and efficient in its operation for packaging.
The above-discussed maturity levels of the commercial capability in packaging is summa-
rized in figure 17
Figure 17: Packaging Maturity Levels Definition
5.3.2
Procurement
According to the findings, procurement is driven mainly by the owner’s ability to identify,
select and motivate suppliers for commercial activities with the organization. Procurement ac-
tivities relate to the organization’s ability to manage supplier selection, bidding, negotiation, and
contract management. In addition, the organization’s activities on how to purchase products,
goods, and services, taking into account in-sourcing or outsourcing methods. The findings of
the interviews induced the latter driver.
At an initial level of maturity in procurement, the organization has very limited suppliers
110
==PAGE:125==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
to choose from when purchasing products, goods, or services. Also, the organization obtains a
flawed tendering system.
On a more defined and organized level of maturity, the organization has many suppliers
capable of delivering products. At the same time, the competitive advantage is shared equally be-
tween the organization and the suppliers. Also, the organization obtains a tendering system where
the selection of the suppliers is based on well-defined criteria. Criteria such as time, cost, quality,
and sustainability play an essential role in the tendering process. At that point, the sustainabil-
ity enabling dimension plays a crucial role in supplier selection. Furthermore, the organization
procures products, goods, or services by ensuring a sustainable-green supply chain. In addition,
the suppliers must provide documentation about changing to a more green sourcing supply in
the upcoming future. Finally, the organization works with contractors who have sufficient re-
sources such as human resources, machinery, and competencies for executing multiple projects
for a particular time.
At a more mature level, the organization has a manageable number of suppliers capable of
delivering specialized products. At the same time, the competitive advantage is shared equally be-
tween the organization and the suppliers. At that point, the strategic partnership enabling dimen-
sion is essential in-between the organization and the key suppliers. Moreover, the organization
possesses the flexibility to choose from a preferred supplier list where the suppliers possess docu-
mented performance. Furthermore, in most cases, the organization procures products, goods, or
services by ensuring a sustainable-green supply chain. In addition, the suppliers require to pro-
vide documentation of their current or future strategy for green sourcing. Likewise, it is essential
that the organization determines the scope of its projects and manages its consultants with the
help of the government and stakeholders. Lastly, the organization works with contractors who
have manageable resources such as human resources, machinery, and competencies for executing
multiple projects for a particular time.
Finally, in an optimized level of maturity, the organization has the optimal number of sup-
pliers capable of delivering specialized products, while the competitive advantage remains on the
organization. Also, the organization possesses the flexibility to choose from a preferred supplier
111
==PAGE:126==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
list where the suppliers possess documented high performance. In addition, the suppliers on the
list obtain high experience, financial strength, and organizational skills. Likewise, the organi-
zation optimal procures products, goods, or services by following the national and international
regulations and being flexible enough to execute projects on time. Furthermore, the organiza-
tion always procures products, goods, or services by ensuring a sustainable-green supply chain.
The suppliers require to provide documentation of their green supply. Moreover, the organiza-
tion optimally determines the scope of its projects and manages its consultants by itself. Last but
not least, the organization works with contractors who have unlimited resources such as human
resources, machinery, and competencies for executing multiple projects for a particular time.

==PAGE:127==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
The above-discussed maturity levels of the commercial capability in procurement is sum-
marized in figure 18
Figure 18: Procurement Maturity Levels Definition
5.3.3
Authority and Legal Processing
According to the primary data findings authority and legal processing is mainly driven by
two CSFs. The first one defines the owners’ knowledge, use, and involvement in the tender
regulations, contracts, or legal agreements to comply with the rules and norms of the national
and international standards. The second one defines the owner’s role in facilitating the approval
of decisions, legal, and authority matters of the project.
113
==PAGE:128==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
At the initial maturity level, the organization has an uncontrolled approach to project com-
pliance according to national building and construction regulations, as the owner struggles with
the complexity behind these regulations. As strategic processing and portfolio management ca-
pabilities mature to a more managed state, the owner organization starts operating according
to a managed set of regulations by the government, as complexity management becomes better
managed at the organization.
In a more organized and defined level of maturity, the owner organization develops a de-
fined contract management approach, and the national and international regulations are firmly
established at the owner organization. Furthermore, as the organization evolves to level four of
maturity, compliance with project requirements’ national and international regulations is mea-
sured and controlled. This is mainly due to the organization’s maturity in utilizing project 13
concepts, systems engineering, and sustainable leadership. Moreover, contract management is
controlled with a well-defined approach but lacks a clear strategy for the various project and con-
tract types. At this maturity stage, approvals made are controlled using a stage-gate model but
not fully complied with due to its effect on project delay.
Finally, in an optimal maturity state, the organization operates by following the national
and international regulations and being flexible enough to execute projects on time. Moreover,
Contract management is optimal, and there is a clear strategy and SOP’s for contract management
specific for each project and contract type. The use of the danish AB18 and ABR18 is facilitating
the work processes. Furthermore, systematic stage-gate model for approvals is established and
well defined at the owner organization.
114
==PAGE:129==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
The above-discussed maturity levels of the governance capability in authority and legal pro-
cessing is summarized in figure 19.
Figure 19: Authority and Legal Processing Maturity Levels Definition
5.3.4
Relational
According to the findings, relationship management is primarily driven by the owner’s abil-
ity to collaborate with commercial parties effectively. In addition, relational capabilities are iden-
tified as vital owner capabilities. Specifically, the work environment in collaboration between
the organization and other project participants is critical. Also, the new way of doing things can
benefit both parties. In addition, the organization’s goal is to get the maximum value for money
that an organization pays to its suppliers. Finally, it is equally vital for the owner organization to
respect the cultural differences between the project participants. The findings of the interviews
induced the latter driver.
At an initial level of maturity in relational, the organization underestimates the value of trust
between project participants. Also, the organization limited the values of the diverse culture in
the organization and society. Also, there is no intention for sharing learning and innovation
promotion.
115
==PAGE:130==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
At a more repeatable level, the organization starts developing trust between the project par-
ticipants, developing to value the diverse culture in the organization and society, and promoting
continuous sharing, learning, and innovation.
On a more defined and organized level of maturity, the organization has a sufficient level
of trust among the participants in the project to share the project’s progress with transparency,
values the diverse culture in the organization, and promotes continuous sharing of learning and
innovation. Last but not least, it is worth noting that it is difficult to achieve a project if at least
a party has lost money or reputation during the life of the project. Therefore, the strategic part-
nership enabling dimension is beginning to be an essential factor for the owners’ organization to
achieve a strong relationship with market suppliers.
At a more mature level, the organization has a high level of trust between the project partic-
ipants to secure manageable project execution and performance transparency. Dashboards moni-
toring and sharing the project performance are shared with all the project participants. However,
only some project parties will be successful upon completion. These could be monetary achieve-
ments, reputation, lessons learned, innovation. Moreover, the organization manageable values
the diverse culture in the organization and the society. Finally, the organization promotes man-
ageable continuous sharing, learning, and innovation.
Finally, in an optimized level of maturity, the organization has optimal trust between the
project participants to secure manageable project execution and performance transparency. Dash-
boards monitoring and sharing the project performance are shared with all the project partic-
ipants. In addition, most issues are resolved between organizations, avoiding going to court.
Moreover, all involved parties to the project will be successful upon completion. These could be
monetary achievements, reputation, lessons learned, innovation. Furthermore, the organization
values the diverse culture in the organization and the society optimally. Last but not least, the
organization promotes the continuous sharing of learning and innovation optimally.

==PAGE:131==

The above-discussed maturity levels of the commercial capability in relational is summarized
in figure 20.
Figure 20: Relational Maturity Levels Definition
5.3.5
Globalization
Globalization was one of the capabilities induced from the analysis of the interviews. The
capability entails that the owner organization must include in its list of suppliers, suppliers that are
also active abroad. The organization must be flexible to work with international suppliers around
the world. Therefore, international cooperation is essential for the organization where cooper-
ation between the owner and international suppliers is defined. At an initial level of maturity in
globalization, the organization focuses only on the national-niche market.
At a more repeatable level, the organization begins to work with international organizations
to increase its network, knowledge, skills, capabilities, machinery. Also, the international suppliers
may be from neighboring countries.
117
==PAGE:132==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
On a more defined and organized level of maturity, the organization works with many
international organizations to increase its network, knowledge, skills, competencies, machinery.
In addition, the organization begins recognizing the advantages of working on a bigger market.
Also, the cooperation between the organization and the supplier can be short-term or long-term.
At a more mature level, the organization collaborates with many international organizations
to increase its network, knowledge, skills, capabilities, machinery. In addition, the organization
begins to sign contracts on a long-term basis. Moreover, the cooperation between the organiza-
tion and the supplier can be flexible between short-term or long-term. At that point, the strategic
partnership takes place to support the organization to work effectively with the suppliers in the
long run. In that way, strong relationships between the owner organization and the suppliers will
occur.
Finally, in an optimized level of maturity, the organization cooperates with many interna-
tional organizations to increase its network, knowledge, skills, capabilities, machinery. It is worth
noting that the international supplier can fill the national supply gap all year round. Last but not
least, the organization benefits from the best prices and quality of deliveries from suppliers.

==PAGE:133==

The above-discussed maturity levels of the commercial capability in globalization is summa-
rized in figure 21.
