5.4
Governance Dimension Maturity Levels Definition
5.4.1
Assurance
As discussed in the findings section, there are four main drivers for assurance. Initially,
the communication between the organization and the temporary project management helps ex-
change information. Second, compliance capabilities operate based on organizational rules and
requirements. Third, the definition of ownership determines the person’s assignment responsible
for an action or activity. Fourth, the owner’s organizational structure defines the owner’s ability
to create an organizational structure that connects all project parts.
At an initial level of maturity in assurance, the organization does not communicate the
project’s progress with its stakeholders. Also, the organization does not define the role of the
project participant.
The organization begins communicating the project’s progress with the stakeholders at a
119
==PAGE:134==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
more repeatable level. Moreover, the organization begins defining the role of the project partic-
ipant. However, there is still no clear definition of the role, and sometimes the participants in the
project do not willingly continue their actions as they can not recognize if they are part of their
mission.
On a more defined and organized level of maturity, the organization communicates the
project’s progress with the stakeholders. The benefits of sharing the project progress with the
stakeholders start creating a solid corporate connection between them. Furthermore, the orga-
nization sufficiently defines the role of the project participant. The role of the participants is doc-
umented under informal contracts. Moreover, the organization sufficiently defines a systematic
model for controlling and monitoring the project’s progress by setting the project’s milestones.
Finally, the major challenge found in that level shows that the organization tends to continue and
move its project to the next phase even though the prerequired phase is not completed yet. That
could indicate a significant risk, but sometimes the risk of stomping the project is even more
considerable. Therefore, at this level, the enabling dimensions project organizing and systems
engineering starts playing a crucial role in developing the assurance capabilities to reach the full
potential.
The organization adequately communicates the project’s progress with stakeholders at a
more mature level. Meetings are organized frequently. The organization also understands the
need to comply with the rules, progress, and procedures to create consistent work procedures.
Also, the organization well-defines the role of the project participant. Likewise, the role of the
participants is documented under formal contracts. Moreover, the organization well-defines a
systematic model for controlling and monitoring the project’s progress. Dashboards are created
to visualize the project progress transparently. Finally, the organization well-identifies and con-
nects the various departments during the project’s life.
Finally, in an optimized level of maturity, the organization optimally communicates the
project’s progress with stakeholders. Flexible meetings are organized frequently. In addition, the
organization creates solid work procedures. Moreover, the organization optimally defines the
role of the project participant and the optimal way of communication between the organization
120
==PAGE:135==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
and the project participants. Every participant’s behavior, role, and skills are documented under
formal contract agreements. Likewise, the organization optimally defines a systematic model for
controlling and monitoring the project’s progress. Dashboards are created to visualize the project
progress transparently. In addition, the organization optimally defines the project’s milestones to
control and monitor the project’s progress. It is worth noting that specialized project management
software tools are utilized to optimize project performance. Finally, the organization optimally
identifies and connects the various departments during the project’s life.
The above-discussed maturity levels of the governance capability in assurance is summarized
in figure 22.
Figure 22: Assurance Maturity Levels Definition
5.4.2
Project Coordination
Project coordination is one of the main governance capabilities that connect with many other
capabilities in the strategic and commercial dimensions. Therefore, having a governed project
121
==PAGE:136==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
coordination process improves stakeholder management, among other capabilities like relational
and packaging capabilities. As discussed in the findings section, project coordination was found
to be driven by three main CSFs. The first is cooperation efficiency which describes the project
participants’ willingness to cooperate and the effect on information exchange. The second one is
responsibility definition which describes the owner organization’s ability to define responsibilities
between all project participants. The last CSF is monitoring and controlling, which refers to the
owner’s effective control and measurement of project performance over its lifetime.
At an initial stage of maturity, it can be argued that the owner organization struggles in
coordinating the different engineering and management project tasks in a project as cooperation
efficiency would be at a growing or immature state. Moreover, project roles and responsibili-
ties would be unclear at this phase due to poor and ineffective communication between project
participants.
As repeatable processes begin to occur at the organization, monitoring and project control
objectives would be established at a project level. It is also argued that project roles and respon-
sibilities are defined at this maturity level, but there would be an apparent absence of culture in
the project team. Moreover, The owner organization would have developed or sourced a project
management team to coordinate the cross-functional roles in a project.
In a more organized and defined maturity level and using the application of active leadership
and talent management, engineering management is defined. The organization is in the early
stages of applying it. Moreover, as the commercial capabilities dimension develops because con-
tracting strategies are in place, the owner considers applying strategic partnerships and project 13
enterprise structure for project coordination. At this level of maturity, actual project status facts
are not very well clear and are very subjective.
As the organization matures to level 4, the owner organization would have a structured
project organization with defined roles through systems engineering application. The project
culture would be at a developing stage. Moreover, the organization would have developed a
specialized competency in engineering management through effective talent training programs
and sustainable leadership. The organization’s resources would have the ability to coordinate
122
==PAGE:137==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
between the different disciplines in the industry. At this level of maturity, digital transformation
and data acquisition start at the organization to monitor project performance and communication
between project parties.
At an optimal level of maturity, the owner organization would constantly be looking into op-
timizing and developing more cooperation efficiency in its projects between the different stake-
holders. Moreover, project stakeholders would have clear roles and responsibilities definitions
for optimal coordination, including a customized project team culture. In addition, data-driven
logging for monitoring and controlling project status and interaction between the project stake-
holders creates trust and transparency.
The above-discussed maturity levels of the governance capability in project coordination is
summarized in figure 23.
Figure 23: Project Coordination Maturity Levels Definition
5.4.3
Asset Management
According to the findings from the literature, asset management addresses the capabilities
required for an organization to integrate the final product or asset into its existing operation.
123
==PAGE:138==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Furthermore, based on the findings from the interviews, it is also important to consider the flex-
ibility of the asset to change over time. As a result, since the ability to manage assets is vital to
the organization, the two main drivers for effectively and efficiently asset management are asset
integration and buildability.
At an initial level of maturity in asset management, the organization acquires poor proce-
dures to hand over a finished asset to other parts of the organization or external organizations.
Also, the organization obtains insufficient data to manage its assets. Finally, the organization
poorly considers the differences between the design and the actual execution phases.
At a more repeatable level, the organization acquires informal procedures to hand over a
finished asset to other parts of the organization or external organizations. Likewise, the organi-
zation obtains sufficient data to manage its assets. Finally, the organization begins considering
the differences between the design and the actual execution phases.
On a more defined and organized level of maturity, the organization acquires sufficient
formal procedures to hand over a finished asset to other parts of the organization or external
organizations. Moreover, the organization possesses accurate data about the asset information.
In addition, the organization sufficiently considers the differences between the design and the
actual execution phases. However, the organization cannot always trust the data to make deci-
sions. Therefore, project organizing, systems engineering, and sustainability are the actuators in
developing further asset management capabilities at this level.
At a more mature level, the organization acquires well-defined formal procedures to hand
over a finished asset to other parts of the organization or external organizations. Also, the or-
ganization possesses sufficient updated and accurate data about the asset information. Likewise,
the organization obtains well-defined procedures to consider the differences between the design
and the actual execution phases. The designers partially consider all the elements and difficulties
where a project can fail, such as weather conditions and resources. In addition, there are business
intelligence systems to support the organization to work with the data sufficiently. Furthermore,
the organization partially considers the data in order to make decisions. Finally, the organization
obtains most ISO certifications for asset management.
124
==PAGE:139==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Finally, in an optimized level of maturity, the organization acquires optimal defined formal
procedures to hand over a finished asset to other parts of the organization or external organi-
zations. Also, the organization possesses updated and accurate data about the asset information.
Moreover, the organization obtains formal, well-defined procedures to consider the differences
between the design and the actual execution phases. The designers consider all the elements and
difficulties where a project can fail, such as weather conditions and resources. In addition, there
are business intelligence systems to support the organization to work with the data optimally.
Furthermore, all the projects are created and documented based on data, and the organization is
responsible for updating the data in the following years. The organization trusts the data to make
decisions based on that. Finally, the organization obtains almost all ISO certifications for asset
management.
The above-discussed maturity levels of the governance capability in asset management is
summarized in figure 24.
Figure 24: Asset Management Maturity Levels Definition

==PAGE:140==

5.5
OCMM Framework
In this section, the final version of OCMM was described and discussed. The OCMM is an
excel-based tool in the form of spreadsheets. In total, there are nineteen spreadsheets. Firstly,
there are sheets, such as introduction and glossary, that provide general guidance to help the
user understand the structure, content, language, and any other concern on how to use the tool.
Moreover, there is an example Questionnaire sheet that can be used to collect information from
the organizations in advance of the assessment. Also, there is an example Assessment Process
to help with stakeholder engagement and planning. On top of that, the users of the tool are
free to modify the material within these tabs to suit their particular needs. Moreover, the other
sheets present the organizational capabilities. In the latter sheets, descriptions of criteria that are
assigned to a particular maturity level for each capability are depicted. Finally, it is worth noting
that there are two more sheets, summary and detail sheets, where the overall assessment ratings
are presented. The main characteristics and differences of those two sheets were further discussed
below.
Starting by describing the main features of the maturity model. In the maturity model, the
x-axis is the dimensions of maturity levels sorted from the lowest to the highest; thus, from Level
one to Level five. At the same time, the y-axis is the capabilities dimensions and the capabilities
that are correlated to those dimensions. It is worth noting that each capability dimension carries a
unique color. Moreover, the bottom line in the model presents the total scoring percentage-wise
per dimension in every maturity level and the total scoring per maturity element. In addition, on
the right-hand side of the model, the total score per capability is depicted. The main difference
between the summary and detail view is the following.
Specifically, in the summary view, the model illustrates the capability maturity assessment
of the organization qualitatively. As it can be seen in the figure 25, in the gray area, the level of
maturity per capability and maturity level is shown based on the following four indicators. The
"None" indicator determines whether the capability definition does not describe the organiza-
tion. The "emerging" indicators shows whether the capability partially describe the organization.
The "Progressive" indicators displays whether the capability describes the organization to a high
126
==PAGE:141==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
extent. Last but not least, the "Mature" indicator illustrates whether the organization has the
optimal level of maturity; thus, the capability fully describes the organization. Therefore, the
summary rating system implements quality assessments that determine whether a certain level
maturity is achieved. In order to do so, the user can scan through the criteria and the described
definition of each capability level, and choose an overall rating such as "partially describes my
capability". The summary view can be seen in the figure 25.
Figure 25: Summary view of the OCMM
In contrast, the detailed view presents the results in the gray area quantitative rather than
qualitative. In that way there is a percentage-wise illustration of the score per capability at every
level. It is worth bearing in mind that the percentage rating system measures the average score
of all criteria for a specific dimension under a maturity level. The detailed view can be seen in
the figure26.
127
==PAGE:142==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
In addition, in order for the user to rate each capability, the detailed scoring system requires
rating each criterion on a scale from 0 to 100, with the scores displayed in Harvey Balls located
next to each individual criterion. Harvey balls indicates the extent to which the criterion has been
met:
• 0-20: The organization has not met or passed the level of this criterion.
• 20-40: The organization meets some of the aspects of this criterion.
• 40-60 The organization partially meets the aspects of this criterion.
• 60-80: The organization meets most of the aspects of this criterion.
• 80-100: The organization has met and passed the level of this criterion.
Figure 26: Detailed view of the OCMM
128
==PAGE:143==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
In conclusion, there are separate spreadsheet tabs ("Summary" and "Detail") to show the
results of the different scoring approaches. Users are free to choose whichever approach best suits
their needs, however, doing both is recommended. The OCMM can be found in the appendix
17
129
==PAGE:144==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
6
Discussions
6.1
Limitations
As the research topic was vast, the authors knew that the research was on an industry-level
base and that the tool is still under further development. Future tool users would still need to tailor
it according to their services field. In addition to that, one major limitation in the project was
the sample of industry experts interviewed. A larger sample would contribute to more flexible
and heterogeneous data where more patterns would be found in the analysis, ultimately leading
to a more credible and accurate assessment tool. However, the researchers still managed to col-
lect industry data from ten experts currently working in seven different owner organizations in
Denmark, given the limited time frame that this thesis dissertation was bounded to.
Moreover, as the methodology presented, the primary data included a sent-out survey in the
early phases of the research where the capability map was still under development. The survey
was designed at the beginning of the study, covering only capabilities and CSFs from iteration 1.
The main intention from the survey was to get an industry perception regarding the prioritization
of the capabilities and CSFs under each dimension. This would have helped create a weighting
scale for each CSF and capability. However, more capabilities and CSF were added during the
second iteration, so the survey’s output was no longer valuable. Therefore, all the capabilities
and CSF had equal weights in this developed model. Thus, the authors’ recommendation will be
to design a new survey following the same procedure as the designed one but including all the
capabilities and CSF of the second iteration. The new survey’s output will help developing the
maturity model to a more credible, accurate, and transparent tool by providing weights for each
CSF and capability.
6.2
Implications on Practice
In this section, the reliability was looked into to clarify whether the framework developed
and the findings addressed in this research are credible and trustworthy. In order to do this, the
authors utilized and tested the OCMM with the Danish Building Construction Client organiza-
130
==PAGE:145==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
tion to assess its capability maturity level performance. The main challenges that occurred by the
Danish Building Construction Client organization to use the model were further discussed.
The results received from the Danish Building Client had some limitations as the informant
was limited to his own personal knowledge when doing the assessment, and the time provided
to full-fill the assessment was quite short (5 days), as the deadline for submitting this research
was near. However, in a case study scenario, experts from different organizational divisions will
be gathered to answer question related to their field of knowledge, and the assessment process
described later in this section will be followed. One of the main findings to be taken into con-
sideration from these results is that it was noticed that in some capabilities, the maturity level 5
seemed unobtainable. An example from ’Packaging’: ’The organization optimally combines or
splits tenders to optimally execute projects on time with the lowest risk, best price, best quality,
sustainability.’ From the informant’s perspective no organization working with complex projects
would be able to score 100 on that – it can be a vision but not a realistic, achievable steady state.
Moreover another crucial finding was that there are some aspects of the evaluation that does
not fit well the context of a public owner organization in the construction industry. An example
from ’Procurement’: ’The organization possesses the flexibility to choose from a preferred supplier
list where the suppliers possess documented high performance. The suppliers on the list obtain
high experience, financial strength, and organizational skills.’ Public owner organizations, cannot
choose their suppliers as they are subject to the legal constraints of European tendering legislation.
This means that they regularly have to settle with mediocre or even poorly performing suppliers.
This means that they legally cannot reach the maximum maturity level on this criteria.
One way to address these challenges could be to allow the organization to set specific orga-
nizational targets for each dimension, so that level 5 is not the aim for all. For some organizations
level 5 would go against the organizational strategy and therefore it would be advised to aim for
that in their processes. For example, if the Danish construction client organization pursued a
level 4 or 5 for globalization that would most likely cost way more resources than they would re-
ceive benefits since their mandate is only to operate in Denmark. By allowing for organizational
specific goals then the assessment can more easily move on from just giving a status to advance
131
==PAGE:146==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
towards informing a tailored road map for improvements.
The developed maturity model could have beneficial implications in the industry in general.
With this maturity assessment tool, the owner organization would have the option and possibil-
ity to assess their performance and capability maturity. Moreover, it would be more convenient
to benchmark organizational needed growth goals with this tool. In addition, the owner or-
ganization could discover the capabilities used by the other organizations in the same industry.
However, it is recommended to gather further secondary data to test and validate the developed
framework at a case company.
The authors recommend the following assessment process visualized in figure 27 when an-
alyzing capability maturity at an owner organization.
Figure 27: Practical Assessment Process
• Preparation and Pre-Work: At this initial assessment phase, the analysts will gather infor-
mation about the owner organization, including reports and key employees filling a ques-
tionnaire. This before-mentioned questionnaire has not yet been developed as it is one of
the next steps the researchers will be working on. The questionnaire will include specific
questions about each capability in the assessment model, facilitating the assessment process.
132
==PAGE:147==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
In some cases, it could also be that tailored questions will be designed to assess a specific
organization.
• Assessment of the owner organization: At this phase, the analysts will review the completed
questionnaire, discuss open questions, and address other outstanding items at the owner
organization. Moreover, the analysts would fill out the OCMM assessment tool as a result
of the answers to the questionnaire.
• Assessment summary: A Summary report and presentation on the current SO maturity
state would be prepared by the analysts, opportunities to move up the maturity scale would
be identified, and recommendations on how to move up would be presented to the man-
agement of the owner organization.
• Road-mapping and prioritization: Based on the results of the assessment summary, a meet-
ing with owner organization leadership would be set to review the results and recommen-
dations. In order endorsement of a prioritization road map would be gained.
• Business case: A business case for the prioritized road map would be developed with the
owner organization personnel.
• Implementation: Finally, implementation of the prioritized road map, including business
case. The project would be approved and funded by the management and leadership of the
owner organization. Lastly, resources would be deployed to execute and track against the
agreed road map.
6.3
Implication on Academia
The latest research and literature about strong owner capabilities has mainly focused on the
needed capabilities at owner organizations to execute projects more successfully. It has also been
revolving around the fundamental industry problems tackled in this research study. However,
not much research has been done on maturity models for the construction industry’s owner ca-
pability development. This is where this research provides its main contribution to the industry.
The maturity model developed, and the area researched in this dissertation entailed many of the
133
==PAGE:148==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
recent work done in the area of owner capabilities, development of capabilities for better project
execution as well as empirical data from the industry practitioners, which only enhanced the
credibility of the results and outcome of this work.
Moreover, this research study has set a baseline and direction for future research. One of the
main topics that could follow this research is the study of the enabling dimensions reviewed in this
research. The enabling dimensions, or so-called enablers, which were reviewed in this research
are systems engineering (SE), project organizing (PO), leadership, and strategic partnerships (SP).
Future research should focus on using these enablers in the developed maturity model, or in other
words, the ”how” of the application of these enablers in theory and practice. These could be
certain practices that owner organizations must do or develop at each level of maturity in each
capability. Furthermore, a review of more enablers could be substantial to develop this model
further.
134
==PAGE:149==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
7
Conclusion
This research study focused mainly on two major topics in the construction industry. The
first was mapping out capabilities argued to be essential for owner organizations to become strong
owners, and the other was developing a maturity model for assessing these capabilities. The con-
ceptual capability framework of (G. Winch & Leiringer, 2016) was central in this research as the
three capability dimensions discussed formed the basis of the developed capability map in this
research. Moreover, recent literature about owner capabilities in the construction industry and
maturity models from several industries were reviewed to create a first iteration of the capability
map and the maturity model. Furthermore, this research entailed empirical assessment besides
the theoretical one, where data was collected through interviews with industry experts to form
a better understanding of the capabilities in practice and challenges in the Danish market, KPI’s,
CSF’s and maturity elements. The findings from the empirical data were analyzed for two pur-
poses. The first purpose was to review the developed capability map to make a second iteration
for the maturity model. The interviews mainly justified the capabilities defined in iteration one,
and more capabilities under the three dimensions were induced such as knowledge management
under strategic and globalization under the commercial pillars. The second purpose was to en-
hance the credibility and reliability of this research and develop the tool so that future researchers
could make greater use of it. In conclusion, this research study aims to benchmark best practices
of each capability, which would ease the green transition of the whole industry.

==PAGE:150==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
8
Project Team Reflections
The project’s master plan was created reflecting all of the project’s main activities, timeline,
milestones, and the critical path for the delivery date. The project’s master plan had been through
several reviews during the project period to reflect on the progress and change of priorities. The
final version can be seen in appendix 18. In order to facilitate the authors’ journey in writing this
master thesis, the following tools and practices were used.
• A project structure on Microsoft Teams project, to support virtual meetings, share relevant
literature, and empirical data was developed.
• Throughout the lifetime of the master thesis, a sequence of weekly meetings was organized
that was held with the Professor and supervisor Christian in 30 min intervals.
• A project action plan was created using excel to track all the project’s activities on a weekly
basis. The activities were assigned in-between the two authors of this master dissertation.
Furthermore, the action responsible reports to the other about the status of the actions,
where then discussions were made on how to follow up on the actions and if the set dead-
line was still realistic. Wrapping up, all the necessary information on how to complete the
action in terms of planning, organizing, leading, and controlling were discussed between
the authors. This helped keep motivation, accountability, a structured and controlled ap-
proach to track the project milestones. The actions plan can be seen in appendix 18.

9.1
Interviews with Danish Railway Client
Interviewers

Team: First we will start with general questions and then we’ll get we’ll dig deeper into capability
questions. So, to start with, the general ones, can you tell us a bit about yourself and and your role within
Danish Railway Client?
Section manager A: I am a section Manager, where I manage a team of project managers.
A team of 10 project managers in a matrix organization so that the rest of the resources for our
projects are managed up elsewhere. My responsibility is a track renewals which is rather large
projects on existing railway. It’s not maintenance, so it’s the next level after the the tracks are not
able to be maintained and we have a need for for renewal.
Team: Based on your position, your experience and point of view, what do you think are the needed
capabilities for project success at Danish Railway Client?
Section manager A: We will need to be able to allow our self to define the scope of these
projects, and to manage our advisors, in a way the projects that will end up tendering out are
143
==PAGE:158==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
contractible and can meet the the quality and financial goals of the project. So that means that we
have to be very strong in the technical skills of our trade. And also when we get to the construction
phase, we should have a construction managers that are able to to execute projects in collaboration
with our suppliers. So I think it’s these two capabilities apart from of course project management
itself.
Team: How to you manage your portfolio in your organization? What are the main criteria and in
managing project portfolio?
Section manager A: It’s two things actually. It’s the economic lifespan of the track and the
financial possibilities in our budget. These are the two factors that we try to balance.
Team: Are project selection, project definition, and data part these criteria as well?
Section manager A: Yes, it’s very data based. Of course.
Team: Do you see any challenges general when it comes to portfolio management in your organiza-
tion?
Section manager A: I think there are many. One thing, it’s impossible to have a change in
one project without conflicting or having impact on the rest of the portfolio because we are all
using the same scarce resources both internally and externally. There’s a real vulnerability in the
portfolio. Also in terms of managing our customers’ interests.
Team: How would you define a successful project portfolio management?
Section manager A: Uh, I’m not sure we really have set up a specific goal to monitor that.
But in my opinion, it would be that we did not have the need to reschedule projects due to
future causes that come out outside of the project. Of course, project can always have the need
for rescheduling for internal reasons, but when we reschedule the whole portfolio because of
impacts from other projects, that means unsuccessful portfolio management.
Team: What are the main criteria, in your opinion, for successful stakeholder management?
144
==PAGE:159==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Section manager A: We have so many stakeholders that I don’t really know where to start.
Beginning with the internal stakeholders first. We have the part of the organization that are
responsible for maintaining our tracks and they would have a wish for a very high quality projects.
And that has to balance with the time that we have for the execution phase and also with the
money. So we are always trying to find the right level of quality in the projects to have a common
understanding of internal stakeholders. That’s very important.
For the external stakeholders, in our case are primarily the operators who drive the trains,
and it can be cargo and it can be passengers. But for them two things are very important. First,
we close tracks as little as possible. Second when we close tracks that we always deliver on time
in accordance with the schedule so that it can be planned in advance which trains we disrupt and
when we will open again. That might lead to a chaotic version which is very bad for our external
stakeholders. So time is always the highest priority, and in Project Triangle, it is always time that
wins.
Team: Would you say decision making and communication are important criteria when it comes to
the stakeholder management?
Section manager A: Yes I do. Actually, for us the stakeholder management is very much
about getting the right circumstances for the project so that it has a chance of success. Because
we always have to push the envelope for the opportunities for the project in a sense, if we can get
the right frames for our project and we can also deliver inside these frames, then it’s a success. So
it’s a two way thing. Without stakeholders, of course, it’s always a negotiation.
Team: Where do you see the main challenges in stakeholder management ?
Section manager A: Well, the main challenges in terms of our external stakeholders occur in
the early phases where the external stakeholders would always have an interest in closing down
the fewest tracks as possible. Then, in the latter stages when you during execution, they will
always want the tracks to open on time. These two points are sometimes contradictory. So we
always have to re-scope to fit the new circumstances so that makes a lot of changes in the projects.
145
==PAGE:160==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Team: Based on your opinion, what are the main criteria of Danish Railway Client to have a sustain-
able capital raising?
Section manager A: Well, we’re 100% publicly financed, so that’s really a political question.
So political factors are definitely impacting our portfolio. At the moment our strategy aims for
low carbon emission, so we have a reasonably substantial funding and not trying to execute within
this because of all the constraints that can be difficult to use enough money. That’s why it’s a kind
of different to approach if you were a private investor, but it’s kind of difficult to do all the projects
within the frames that we have to do work to work in.
Team: Is strategic planning a factor when it comes to capital raising?
Section manager A:Yes, it is. Because we are 100% publicly financed. It can sometimes be
difficult to have a far enough horizon in our decision making. But that’s not the problem at the
moment. I think our Danish politicians have been very forward looking and we can plan for a
really long horizon right now.
Team: Does Danish Railway Client make their own decisions or the decisions coming mainly from
the politicians? How would you define successful capital raising? Do you contribute on these decisions as
an owner organization?
Section manager A: I think maybe some of of the issues could be that publicly financed
projects are often also very tightly governed so that you can’t really. But if there’s no flexibility
in the budgets, they have to be used for what they are awarded for. That can sometimes be a
challenge. But it’s not a real issue. I believe there is a very smooth operation in the interactions
with our government.
Team: Do you see any other strategic capabilities?
Section manager A: No, I don’t think so.
Team: what are the main criteria for successful packaging? Do you have any specific contracting
strategy and interfaces between those work packages that you define?
146
==PAGE:161==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Section manager A: We have several different models that we try to apply, but we tend to
combine tenders more, because as I mentioned we have usually very tight time constraints in our
execution phase of our projects. So it’s not really possible for us to coordinate different suppliers
into our projects. So we try to combine the tenders to have large suppliers that supply everything.
Of course they can use subcontractors, but then they need to have the capability to move around
with all these different suppliers. I think maybe we could tender in smaller packages or specific
packages but then we would need more time.
Team: Would you think it’s better if you have smaller packages?
Section manager A: I think in terms of price it would probably be better, but in terms of
time it would be worse. As I said in the triangle, time always wins with us because every time we
need another day is a day where there’s no trains. And if you take into account the Society cost
of closing the railway is enormous. So we can afford to maybe pay a little bit more in the direct
costs of the projects to avoid these track closures. That’s why we combine the tenders in really
large packages.
Team: What are the main challenges when it comes to smaller packages?
Section manager A: The main challenges concern the time schedule of the projects. If we
want smaller packages, then we always have to separate them in time or geography. We have to
work all at the same time and at the same place. Then we also need the management on site to
be the same.
Team: What are the criteria that you use when you decide on working with a specific supplier for a
certain tender and work package?
Section manager A: It’s always more or less supplier’s experience with similar projects. We
have a system of tendering where we have a list of qualified companies. A supplier should be on
the qualified list in order to participate in the tendering. This is also related with bidding. It’s the
way, of course we can also use public tenders once in a while where the companies that are not
on the list can tender, but then they would have to meet criteria where they can document their
147
==PAGE:162==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
experience and financial strength and organizational capabilities etc. But in most cases we tender
to the qualified list.
Team: Are experience and the quality the main criteria to choose among the suppliers? Are these
criteria more important than the price when it come to the selection of the supplier?
Section manager A: In the actual tender it will always be a combination of many criteria.
Almost always a combination of price and other sub criteria that could be other capabilities in
planning or organizing capabilities where we look at what kind of project managers and different
kind of managers that the organization can provide and what are their competences or using their
experience. But of course, price will always be a major factor in the public tender and we have to
be able to answer for the use of public funds, but we also take into consideration the other factors.
Team: Do you believe this is the most successful way of doing tendering or do you see it can be done
in a more optimal way?
Section manager A: I think this is very regulated by law. So we don’t have too many other
options. Of course, there the possibility tendering by negotiation, but it’s also very, very strictly
governed process. And I think we don’t have too much experience with that in Danish Railway
Client. We tend to do it the more traditional way with a tender that its price combined with
other sub criteria.
Team: Have you tried a strategic partnership with a supplier in certain projects in the past?
Section manager A: I think that would be kind of difficult to do in a public tendering situ-
ation, but we have tried tenders with early involvement where we took partner if you would say
that as supplier in earlier phases and they could affect the project.
Team: What are the main criteria for a successful relational supplier management?
Section manager A: That’s where the contract or the construction management comes into
play, mainly because that’s the role of the construction managers to build a relationship that is both
148
==PAGE:163==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
transparent so that we can have a good documentation for what’s being done. For example, the
quality must be documented, and of course the consumption of our funds must be documented,
but it must also happen in an environment of trust and mutual cooperation because of the time
scale, we can’t afford to go into conflict because things tend to stand still or be less efficient. If
you’re in a conflict situation,so that it is very important to us, and I think also something that we
can improve on.
Team: Where do you see the main challenges in the relational supplier management?
Section manager A: It’s actually a resource constraint, because these employees are not very
easy to find. The ones that really balance all these things that I just mentioned. I think they
are also in demand in, and around us with our suppliers and also with others in the businesses.
That’s a resource issue at the moment. Also, employment rate in Denmark is also very high at
the moment, so it’s a difficult position to fill.
Team: Do you see any other commercial capability?
Section manager A: I think yes. I think there is an issue with the business itself. It’s a kind of
a niche so there are not too many suppliers. There’s also some shortages in in specific machinery
and specific competencies, so that sometimes we can experience a very, very poor competition
on some of our projects, and it’s a very difficult thing to work with on short term it’s impossible.
Maybe in the long term it’s something that we can affect, but it’s not easy. So we are in a market
that’s a bit too narrow.
Team: Are there certain capabilities from a supplier perspective that are missing from the market which

Section manager A: Yep, it’s really difficult to scale, maybe because the market is geared for
one or two projects a year you cannot do more, for instance 4 because the resources are not there.
It is a very specific, very high technical level of delivery so it will take many years for the market
to adapt.

Team: what do you think are the main criteria for successful project coordination according to the
project lifespan?
Section manager A: I think our projects are very technically advanced or very complex tech-
nically. Also, there are lots of different technical competences involved in scoping and projecting
or doing the actual design plans that are to be constructed after. So there’s a lot of coordina-
tion going on between the different types of engineering skills involved in such a project. They
can never stand alone when a change in one will affect the other. So we really need a some
skilled project management or design management that can look across all these different skills
and make the right decisions and the right design choices early, So that we don’t have too many
roundabouts. And that’s a very important competency.
Team: Does that mean that the design manager or the project management should be able to have

later on because we missed something or there was a conflict between two skills that we didn’t
observe early enough.

on a more fluent basis. On a weekly basis we have a board meetings. But where we only go in,
we go into the the milestones that are either delayed or in risk of being delayed, but everything
else is is not going into only pool will be looked into advocates.

buildings and when they then have a problem with something that relates to the daily operations,
then they call our maintenance Department and they fix it and on top of that our maintenance
department are then also responsible for making sure that all the things that are not related to
daily operations, but long term maintenance at that these things are fixed and because we are
in a highly regulated system, then when we get rent for my building from there very first day
we get rent from a building. A small portion of that money is set on a bank account for future
maintenance. So the first time that you need to change or make a some kind of structural change,
changing the roof. Anything like that then you would have a lot of money set aside for that for
that specific building. So then you just start our construction project on that.
Team: What other governance capabilities do you seek from suppliers or consultants when you are
working on a project?
Industrial PhD: Yeah, I think coordination is of huge importance and is undervalued some-

Team: From your experience, Hans, what do you think is the main difference between normal ten-
dering contracting and strategic partnership? Which one would you prefer?
Senior Manager: If you have the right set up, if you have the right organization, the top
management, and you have the right portfolio and then the last thing is maybe the most impor-
tant, because talking about a strategic partnerships with one or two unique individual projects
doesn’t give any meaning in this setup, but if you have the right number of projects over the
coming years then for sure I would recommend strategic partnership. But of course you need to
have the right concept to choose the right partner to have open process where you can develop
both organizations and improve together.
I would say the EU Regulation Tender Regulation says that four years is the maximum and
then if you look into some of these strategic partnerships tendered in Denmark, they have had
four years plus two years. But my a legal advisor said, those two years for an option is not legal
actually, so they said only four years for me, and I think that’s a pity. Actually, if I was in the
private sector, if I was Toyota and should go for a subcontractor or supplier. Why should I go
only for four years I would go for 30 years maybe. But of course, with the opportunity to say no

==PAGE:229==

and to quit them because they were not in a way successful or they couldn’t bring down costs,
or that it couldn’t find the right people whatever, then I would have some opportunities to say
goodbye to them, but I would have a set up where as long as they deliver I would have them
very close to me because, what is Toyota? I I don’t know Toyota, maybe they don’t even make
their own engine. Let’s say then you are really rely on the on such a subcontractor producing the
engine or the wheels or whatever. All the parts of the car, and you need to have a long travel with
them. and four years that’s not enough. You are actually, allowed to have make longer contracts,
but then you need to be very, very precise in describing your projects, and often as a client you
can’t describe,for instance, you tendered out a contract to maintain ”Storbeltsforbindelsen”Then
you could make a 30 year contract because there you could very precisely say that OK, we have
”Storbelt” You should maintain this and this, and it’s a 30 years contract that would be legal.
But when you can’t specify your projects and we couldn’t Danish Regional Client, because as
I explained before, projects kept on coming in and so on, and we couldn’t guarantee a specific
number of projects or specific type of project specific costs level or so, then you are only allowed
to tender a framework contract and there is a maximum of four years and some legal advisers
say you are allowed to take extra two years, but even six years might be too short and that’s a
disadvantage going for a strategic partnership when it takes some time to make all the small things
as you see go together.
Team: How do you see it successful? What are the main criteria there that is important for having a

about that. mistakes can come and failures and some understanding approach up on that, and

make the same failure again? How can we improve together? And they should also ask the same
question to me. How can I improve my business? Because clients also make mistakes.
That’s the big difference because in the normal set up, you don’t have this discussion. You
don’t have this a long term view on the portfolio. You only have your project contract and there
you look details down. Do you deliver or do you not deliver? Do you deliver then it’s fine. You

Team: Before we start with questions about the capabilities, maybe we can start by you telling us a
little bit about yourself and your role in Copenhagen principality?
Senior Manager: I work at ”Byggeriet københavn” which Copenhagen Buildings in English

ity, so it’s ordered actually, not directly by the politician. We have 7 organization inside the
municipality. For instance, there is an organization that works with the children.Both in the
kindergartens and schools and so forth. And they order a lot of schools and kindergartens and
renovations of old buildings and stuff like that. It could also be another organization is the cultural
organization that orders you know tennis facilities or cultural halls or stuff like that, So they are,
delivering those services for the citizens on the cultural side. So there are seven of these orga-
nizations inside the municipality. And there is also a what we call economical organization and
”Buildings copenhagen” My organization is part of that. So we build for all the other six, and so
they have ideas. of what we should build, but only if the politicians in Copenhagen says yes, then
they will send it by our organization and we will make a project.
The size of our portfolio 1.6 billion DKK a year that we build, and around 50 parallel projects
is ongoing overtime.
I can shortly tell that we are 100 people in this organization and we are four that have my

the economy part and then we have a small development group also that goes, you know across

Team: How would you define a successful tender strategy?
Senior Manager: We always make what we call a tender strategy for every project. I think it
starts with us making the tender strategy for the actual project. When we sit together our lawyer
or project leader and me as a leader and discuss what are the risks in this project? What? What is
the real content? What are the success criteria is? How do we want to bring this out to get the
best process for the project, but also the best product in the end. So, there’s a lot of focus put into
that actually to take the when necessary discussions in the planning phase of the project to get
this right. Of course, as I said earlier when this strategy points out that this project is not going
out on its own tender, But we put it into a strategic partnership. Then it’s often because it’s kind

done because we’ve made a lot of similar projects together before, so both the organization is all
tuned in and we’re trying to build that project together. But if it’s a project that concerns some
odd functionality so we don’t have the repetition effect. Then it’s often chosen that we make a
separate tender.

Team: Could you please tell us about yourself?
Senior Manager: My background is that I am trained in Civil engineering and I’m being
working both as a contractor and a client for the last 30 years.
Actually, I could divide it into maybe 10-15 years as a contractor, design, build contractor
for large projects, and then the rest as a client, both private and public clients. My role today in
region capital is that I am the Vice President of the client organization, for large projects. That’s
mean I have responsibility to direct five large super hospitals and in indirect 3-4 minor projects.
I have it organization consisting of around 85 project managers or staff in the client organization
and the portfolio is around 13 billion Danish kroner for the time being.
And I’ve been working in this role for 1.5 year. Previously I worked at DTU at the campus
service where I developed both organization and the governance structures by using Prince 2
models and so forth.

interfaces between partners. The origin of the problems it’s often lack of communication skills.

Team: Could you please tell us about your background?
Director: Yes, I’ve been with Danish Owners Association for 18 years. I’ve been there almost
from the beginning and what we try to do is of course to provide service to our members. We do it
basically in three different ways in terms of the regulation, that sort of defines what you can build
and how you can act as a professional client. Then, we provide a competence development. Lastly,
we have something we call development of projects where we engage in sort of developing new
tools for our members, either that they come themselves with some needs and we start a project
or if we can see across a range of members, that there is a need for new concept or some analysis.
For instance, the report you have read about the need for competencies in value creation was
sort of one of the typical projects we will do, where we ask a Foundation could see something
in this, or could you provide some funding for this type of project and then we engage with our
members and interview them and they had to take part in the project and we come out with a
report, some recommendations and a tool that they can use. These are sort of typical things that
we do in the association.
Team: What do you think are the main criteria for managing the portfolio of projects successfully in
organizations in the construction sector? What are the main challenges that members are facing when it
comes to project portfolio?
Director: I guess one of the challenges at least is the diversity of projects at a typical member

usage of run one room to another up to a project for billions of DKK, as a wide diversity in
241
==PAGE:256==

what they actually undertake in terms of different projects. Adding to this is that the volume of a
project activity also varies over time very much so in one year they will have a volume of maybe
just 100 million and next year, they will have a 800 million of project volume and it’s very hard
to build a professional organization that sort of is able to do all the things that you want to do if
you don’t have a sort of a time horizon of more than than one or two years.
We have examples of the organizations that have succeeded in doing this, for instance DTU,
they had sort of a master plan of renewing an entire campus over 10 years and in such perspective
it’s much easier to build the right capability in your organization.
The last thing, I think we would like to mention is that surrounding this is of course the
governance in our average member organization, there’s only around 10 employees who define
themselves as professional client people. That could be in a municipality, for instance, so they are
group of 10 people overseeing construction projects, but within the municipality they are 10s of
thousands of employees doing other things, and they sort of have to compete for resources with
all of the other areas of activity in in this area. So this is also making it challenging to establish a
truly professional construction organization.

tool the factual understanding on what is going on in a project that would be a giant step, I think.
Because people are often getting into fights because nobody knows what the fact is, so you’re
just presenting your own view and an encounter answer to that view. So I guess that would be a
really nice step.

10
Interview Questions
Interview Questions – Guide
For the record, do you give your consent to be recorded?
We are a part of a research team at DTU working on a thesis project focusing on Strong
Owner Capabilities development in organizations within the infrastructure and construction in-
dustry. We are very interested in learning about your experience and priorities regarding your
current understanding and vision of strong owner capabilities. By owner we mean organization
that owns the projects that they want to execute. A strong owner will ultimately be an organi-
zation having mature level of capabilities to execute projects successfully either independently or
in collaboration with other suppliers.
General Questions and Problem Definition
Q1. Can you tell us a little bit about yourself and your role within your organization?
Q2. In few words and from your experience and point of view. What are the capabilities
needed for project success at your organization?
Intro to Capabilities Dimensions (Strategic, Commercial and Governance)
Commercial: Commercial capabilities are market capabilities needed for the owner to inter-
act with the market suppliers and have relevant contracting strategy, and relations management,
when it comes to dealing with suppliers in the market.
Governance: Governance capabilities are mainly those organizational competencies required
in order for a strong owner organization to interface successfully with the stakeholders of the
project.
Strategic: Strategic capabilities are those defined, controlled and owned by the owner organi-
zation. Those capabilities are activities which are the full responsibility of the owner organization
as they relate to the business and corporate strategy of the organization.
252
==PAGE:267==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
We will start with strategic capabilities questions:
Strategic Capabilities and their maturity elements related questions
Q3. In your organization. What are the main criteria in managing the project portfolio? Is
project selection and project definition a part of this criteria? What are the main challenges?
Q4. How would you define a successful project portfolio management?
Q5. What are the main criteria for successful stakeholder management? Is decision making
and communication important criteria? What are the main challenges?
Q6. What are the main criteria for sustainable capital raising for projects? Is strategic plan-
ning an important factor? What are the key bottlenecks when it comes to capital raising for an
appraisal project? How would you define a successful capital raising?
Q7. What other strategic capabilities do you seek from suppliers or consultants when you
are working on a project?
Add maturity question

when it comes to dealing with suppliers in the market.
